'.Something New in Sewing Machines!
What is there Special about it?
The restless march of invention has enabled us
to bring before the, public improvements of im-
mense value, to some short account of which we
beg your attention for a few moments. Our call-
ing is to relieve the home-mother of all that may
weary her in sewing, and to make the same
throughout a pleasure. That we have during the
last ten years succeeded in so doing, scores of
thousands will testify. And to-day we are supply-
ing a machine whose range of work is absolutely
complete, transcending the hopes and dreams of
years. The construction and form are such as to
place it altogether alxn c and beyond competition,
and for the truth of which assertions, as of the
following, we humbly request that your own eyes
may be the judges.
THE IMPROVED HIGH ARM. A great
desideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sown to pass freely underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well-known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion. .
ALAR GER RANGE OF APPLIANGES is
provided than with any other machine. Samples
of work performed upon these we are prepared at
all times to submit, whilst an elaborate Instruction
Book is supplied, which is alone > sufficient to
explain their uses and their applicability to every
requirement
NICKEL-PLATING.—In our climate the
exposed parts of the machine, and the appliances,
need to be protected from rust. We have, at
considerable expense, added a handsomely nickeled
wheel, nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-
bar and cones, heightening the appearance. These
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A MUCH LARGER DRAWER has taken the
place of the cramped and clumsy contrivance of
former years. When closed it securely fastens the
cover, and is provided with a lock and key.
THE WOODWORK is handsomer than ever,
being always of selected and inlaid Walnut. All
of it has been exposed for weeks in the drying-
room of the Factory to a temperature reaching as
high as 170 degrees ; thus when kept out of damp
and sunshine, it will endure our most trying
climates.
A SELF-THREADING SHUTTLE of solid
steel, saving the trouble of threading by the end
of the cotton. It has an excellent tension, is
strong and simple.
THE BOBIN WINDER is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by itself,
as evenly as upon the real whence it came, and
stopping of its own accord when the bobin is full.
The baby’s tiny clothing, upon which mothers love to
display their choicest handiwork; the boy s tweed suit,
the drawing-room carpet, may all be stitched by the won-
derful BEALE Machine. Examples of all kinds of sewing
required in dresses, millinery, underclothing, tailoring and
upholstery will be shown and explained at our offices free
of charge.
With the wonderful BEALE Machine, whether for hand
or foot use, you can hem any width, make double seams
o” fells, braid, stitch with chenille, Berlin wool or gold
cord, and such like for fancy work; you may make a
pretty lace stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats,
or carpets; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or niching : quilt, weave your own braid
with any combination of colours, and stitch it on at the
same time; make thick woollen door mats, embroidered
antimacassars, window curtains, and so forth; you can
■rform the fashionable shirring or
easily and rapidly m
gathering so popular for sleev es. bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small cords in
hoods and stays: do large cording; gather with or without
a band, and generally do all kinds of st might sewing upon
any material whatever. You can darn upon any woven
goods, and do crewel work. You may make button and
eyelet holes, do overcasting, make herring-bone stitch
upon any materials. This work is absolutely unique, and
can only he accomplished upon the BEALE Patent
Machine.
Any of these performances will be show at our shops
at any time. The BEALE Patent makes a, lock-stitch
which cannot ravel, yet by a momentary alteration the
sewing can be done so as to rip from end to end if desired.
This peculiarity is sometimes useful when children’s clothes
are intended to be altered
We have a special word for husbands: When Hie day’s
business is over, and the evening meal is finished, the
wife—and perhaps the sister or daughter sits down to her
sewing. With her hands alone she cannot, as aforesaid,
do anything like the whole of the work required. In ft
corner of the room is perhaps an old-fashioned machine,
with which she may, by hard driving, occasionally stitch,
with a noise like a sausage maehilje. a simple straight
seam ; hut that is no great help. Sin might to he able to
do ALL the work with it, short of stitching on buttons,
which is a trifle. \nd we have known some instances
■where the lady of the house is anxious to obtain a first-
class machine, that which will he the greatest ol all helps
to her. but the husband demurs on the score (If expense.
Why, it is the only article of the household furnishing
which will ever return you any part of the cost! If the
lady will use it, it MI ST earn far more than the half -a-
crovvn a week necessary to purchase i*. On one little
garment she will save t hat sum, so that in addition to in-
creased comfort your household expenses will not he en-
larged, but ought to he diminished, by the possession of a
BEALE PATENT MACHINE. How often do we waste
money upon really useless knick-knacks, or upon need-
lessly expensive articles and indulgences? The Machine
should faV more than pay for itself during the time you
are clearing it off, and it will then become your own, earn-
ing or saving money as before. Is it not fairlv to ho
called a PltOl TTA BLE IN VEX T,M EN T ?

Medical.
DR. SPEERS
AMERICAN DISPENSARY
31 Y ork Street.
P.O. Box 920.
Dr. Speer is a regular Graduated Physician, educated at
Harvard College, U.S. (for confirmation see New Zealand
Gazette, January 21st, 1886; also New South Wales
Gazette, April 17th, 1888). The doctor has devoted 14
years to the study of CHRONIC, NERVOUS, AND
SPECIAL DISEASES. Four of these were in the leading
hospitals of the United States, which enables him to treat
all complaints with excellent results. All applying to
him will receive his honest opinion of their complaints.
No experimenting; no Mercury used. He will guarantee
a positive cure in every case he undertakes, or forfeit
£2OO. His reason for doing so is this—when there is no
organic disease, and nothing but an inorganic trouble, or,
in other words, a conglomeration of symptoms producing
a certain result, there is no reason why it cannot be re-
moved with the proper remedies, therefore he guarantees.
He wishes it distinctly understood that he does not claim
to perform impossibilities, or to have miraculous power
he claims only to be a skilled and successful physician
thoroughly informed in his speciality—CHßONlC and
SPECIAL DISEASES OF MEN AND WOMEN. People
residing at a distance can, by explaining their troubles in
detail be treated at home. The course of treatment will
not in any way interfere with their business. Examina-
tion and advice free to all. Charges moderate. H. J.
Speer, M D., 31 York-street, Sydney. Post Office Box,
No. 920. Office hours, 10 a.m. 012 a.m. 2to 4 and 7
to 8 p.m. Sundays from 10 to 11 a.m. only.

TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS. TREATMENT, AND CURE.
THE directions contained in this little treatise will b
J- found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if the-
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervon
or Sexual Debility, JVo Medicines will be required, as. In
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure aim
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indi
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and Ion,;
and successful experience in the treatment of disease-,
and contains a fund of useful information to every sui
ferer from the following complaints, viz.;—Nervon-
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Diseontentednes-
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CUBE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITV
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUAR ANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher's treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike an\
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, ami
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifyin

will he forwarded on receipt of two stamps to cover

THE AUSTRALIAN EAGLE.
IF THERE RE A LION IX THE WAY OF “FEDERATION UNDER THE CROWN,” THERE IS ALSO AN RAGLE—HEAR HIS FIRST SCREAM.
“He believed in the union of Australia and the founding of an Australian nation, and he spoke the mind of a grat many members in thar House. (Opposition
cheers.) If they were to depart from their present Constitution let it be to have a flag of their own under an independent Constitution as the United States of Australia. (Oppsi-
tion cheers.) He held these opinions as a native of the soil and he claimed that they were as well entitled to be considered as those of the Premier, who was not a native of the
soil. The time was not ripe for separation yet, and he would not be one to do anything to bring about a severance at the present time. (Hear, hear.") This country had one of
the grandest futures that any country in the world eon Id possess. That future was to be built up by the creation of a new order of things under the flag of the United States of
Australia.” (Cheers.)-Dibbs' Speech.

SATURDAY, MAY 24. 1890.
The Dignity of Labour.
Tnr. ‘’ dignity of labour ” is nno of the old,
weary, worn-out aphorisms of which tin l
world is beginning to grow tired. It has
been preached for so many centuries by men
who avoid labour as if it was a venomous
thing that the venerable saying has gradually
been discredited, and any force it once pos
sessed has been irretrievably lost ; and the
masses on whom this dignity has been too
exclusively conferred have begun to ask why
t he burdens of mere animal toil should bo so
unevenly distributed. Labour, no doubt,
has a dignit y of its own, but except in theory
the world declines to recognise the circum-
stance, and even in the most democratic
communities the man who digs a well in a
thirsty land is of no account by comparison
with the man who leads an army, and the
person who fasts for 40 days is of vastly
greater importance, in the popular estimate,
than the public benefactor who grows a tree
•whore no tree grew before. And in the
greater part both of the civilised and the un-
civilised world the visible dignity of labour
consists only of a vested interest in a broken
chair, and a leaky roof, and a table that is
foundered on three legs and rheumatic on
the fourth ; and embodies a hereditary right
t<; be something lower than an ass and a
shade less dignified than a horse ; and the
outward symbol of the grandeur of toil is the
privilege of turning the weary mill of human
industry from early dawn till dark, and
listening to the ceaseless, changeless, endless
clank and clang of the wheels that are for
ever grinding out gold for the benefit of men
who never labour, and who. consequently
in theory, at least—possess no dignity.
Labour, too, is the only earthly distinction
to which no ambitious minds seem to aspire.
The man who has deserved well of his
country is'not, as a rule, rewarded by a
patent of nobility which confers on him and
his peers the privilege of carrying bricks till
the end of time, nor is the individual whom
t he nation delights to honour promoted to
turn the handle of a grindstone as a mark, of
universal esteem and veneration. Toil is
universally declared to he an honour, and
as universally held to be a degradation, or,
at the least, a necessary and unavoidable evil,
and all the accumulated cant of ages lias
hardly served to alter in the slightest degree
the world’s unanimous verdict.
* # #
The Church, as the faithful ally of wealth,
lias never ceased to proclaim the blessedness
of toil, and to denounce inglorious case as a
curse invented by the enemy of mankind
for the destruction of the human race, but if
this theory.be true, then the Church herself
is an empty lie built upon a foundation of
straw. For while preaching the grandeur
and nobility of servile toil and the loveliness
of poverty, she points oul as the goal of
human happiness a heaven which is tilled
with crowns of gold and where the elect
shall enter into eternal rest, and the ecclesi-
astic is not yet built who w ould dare to teach
the existence of a future state where clanking
wheels are for ever revolving among the
unfading bowers of Paradise, and everlasting
steam-engines groan and whirr and rattle
in ceaseless discord by the shores of the
Jasper Rea, abd the innumerable multitude
of the saints enjoy; in a wider and more
lavish settle, the blessings of labour, of which
t hey received only a foretaste here on earth.
'Pirn paradise of the Church is a land of
palaces- a region of boundless magnificence
and wealth that passes all lin.itcomprehen-
sion- a dreamy, lot us-eating"laud of eternal
summer, where all toil is ended, and the
blessings of poverty enter not ; and so long
as the acme of the Christian’s felicity is an
existence of never-ending repose amid the
roses and fountains of Elysium, the earthly
picture of the joys,,of animal toil and weari-
ness and drudgery and w ant must needs wear
a strangely incongruous aspect, which even
the half-blind eye of faith cannot fail to
recognise. . ...
» * *■
And thus it comes to pass that the world
refuses to regard labour as a blessing, and
the great object of' science and invention is
to render toil practically needless, and to
make steam and electricity take the place of
human exertion in every possible branch of
industry. Everywhere the tireless arms of
iron and steel are making man himself more
and more superfluous except as an intelligent
being who is qualified to oversee the
mechanism of the world, and possibly a time
may bp approaching when even his super-
vision will be in a great measure unnecessary
and the mysterious motive powers which
Nature lias supplied will run the globe by
themselves. But while every effort is being
made to render labour a mere useless custom
banded down from the improgressive ages
when man. through his own ignorance, was a
beast of burden, the old, worn-out story
about the loveliness of toil being in itself
an essential cardinal virtue has lost none of
its pristine freshness. Because man was the
only known motive power in an era of
primitive darkness the theory gradually arose
that he must continue to drag ponderous
burdens behind him through all the cycles of
time, and labour became a species of fetich
to which the obi-men of the church pro-
fessed to bow down, just as the not much
less intelligent obi-man of the Senegal or the
Gambia calls on his followers to bow down to
the equally intelligible god of his devotion.
In practice toil was regarded as a curse—in
theory there was held to be some shadowy
spiritual benefit which accrued through a
ceaseless physical effort, devoted to digging
ditches or hauling bricks or propelling heavy
burdens generally, and the “dignity of
labour” was the common phrase by which
was expressed t he sentiment that man, being
made in God’s own image, was eminently
fitted to drag half-a-ton of bricks uphill and
profit by the exorcise.
#, * *
Therefore man, in his capacity as an intel-
ligent being, went on with that process of
invention which was steadily making himself
more and more superfluous ; and man in his
other capacity as an insensate beast of bur-
den went on toiling because be was unable to
learn that toil was no more an abstract virtue
than baldness or cold feet or any other of the
incidents or accidents of life : and, as the
natural consequence of fhis infatuation, a
wildly anomalous industrial condition has
arisen throughout the civilised world. Over
nine-tenths of the globe the hours of labour
are practically the same as in the days when
man was himself the motive power of indus-
try, and yet the worker of to-day, with the
appliances at his command, produces at least
ten times as much as in the days of old. A
considerable part of this extra product, for-
tunately, is absorbed by the steady increase
of luxury ; the balance serves to glut the
world s markets, and produce a state of
industrial paralysis which makes man the
slave of capital, and renders the chronic
poverty of the masses ten times more unen-
durable and more incurable than of old. The
European or American or Asiatic labourer,
who works year after year for twelve hours
per diem, is the living embodiment of the
great virtue of industry. The product, of his
excessive toil is sufficient to supply the re-
quirements of two men instead of one, and
therefore some other man remains unem-
ployed, and his frantic competition for the
means of sustaining existence forces his rival
to renewed efforts to undersell him, and so
keep his place in the ranks of the great army
of toil. Labour itself becomes the enemy of
labour,-and-the industry and energy of man
bid fair to, till the world with hunger and
and hopeless misery : and yet the old
-theory*that work itself is necessarily virtue,
and that perspiration and dust and exertion
arc intimately associated with moral well-
being. has lost but little of its old-time fresh-
ness. and the “dignity of labour” goes on
for ever in an age when labour is becoming
every day more and more unnecessary.
# * #
labour,- regarded as an abstract virtue, is
a failure. The measure of mini’s industry
should Ire merely the measure of man's re-
quirements, and work, -in an ideal State
must be considered merely as a burden, more
or less grievous,, to be .divided as equally as
possible among the community at large.
Toil in itself is no more a blessing than its
familiar twin-brother poverty, and excessive
toil only lends to greater destitution among
the toilers. The sudden rise of an inter-
national eight-hour movement in Europe and
America is a sign that tire sons of labour
have at last recognised that the everywhere
over-lengthy hours of the labourer is one
of the great causes of that frantic competi-
tion which has made the worker the slave of
his own necessities. But the establishment
of a universal eight-hour system is by no
means the end of the problem, unless, indeed,
invention'has reached its final limits, and
the progress of labour-saving machinery is
to go no further. If in another decade steam
and electricity have doubled or trebled the
jinwer of production, then the hours of
labour must undergo a further diminution,
and if the endless march of invention ultim-
ately brings about an era -when steam and
electricity will manage the world alone, then
man must practically cease to labour, and
tire vaunted virtue of human exertion must
become extinct like the Mammoth and the
Till tec, and many another institution which
was of value in its time and then died away
when its usefulness became a thing of the
past.
. * * *
At present the world is suffering from too
much industry. The starving operative in
the cotton-factories of India toils unceasingly
for 12 or 14 or 16 hours daily to glut an
already glutted market, and, as prices
steadily diminish through over-production,
wages—already at starvation point—keep on
falling, and then the toiler nerves himself to
renewed efforts, and works still harder than
before to make up for the decrease in the
value of his exertions, and by his renewed
struggles the over-crowded markets only
grow still more over-crowded, and the mur-
derous strain of industrial competition is
further intensified. Similarly the starving
farmer of Kansas or Dakota spends his
miserable life in a ceaseless effort to grow
corn for a world which already grows more
corn than it can consume, and when the net
value of his produce sinks to live cents a
bushel his only resource is to grow more and
st : ll more corn, because he still believes that
industry is the road to affluence, and, more-
over, growing corn has become a fixed habit
which it is practically impossible to shake
off. Of course we use the term “over-pro-
duction” merely in a relative sense : were
all the wants of the starving Indian opera-
tives and American farmers supplied like
those of the starving millions elsewhere, then
there would be no cry out about glutted
markets. The hours of labour are not only
too lengthy, but the labourers also are
actually too few, for were access to natural
opportunities easy everywhere, then no
man need starve : no man hog leave to
toil. Yet man goes on in an endless circle
until his very dreams are dreams of cotton
and corn, and toil becomes merely a slow
process of national suicide, and the human
race grows impoverished and worn-out
through the effects of its own unreasoning
vigour. It is for this reason that we pro-
nounce the “ dignity of labour to be
a much overrated institution. Labour
is dignified where it supplies the world’s
requirements and still leaves to the
labourer the status of a reasoning animal,
but labour under the conditions which pre-
vail over nine-tenths of the earth is merely
an insane device whereby one man is induced
to do the work of fwo.in order that the other
man may be forced to undersell him in a
steadily increasing ratio of misery and degra-
dation ; and in these circumstances it
possesses no more innate dignity than the
toils of Sisyphus, condemned to roll a pon-
derous stone uphill through all eternity and
yet never reach the summit. And in one
respect even that doomed toiler was ahead of
the labourer of Asia and Europe, for he was
harassed by no starving competitors driven
by the pressure of monopoly to compete
with him in his highly-taxed industry till the
stone grew larger and the hill higher and the
eternity of unprofitable exertion still longer
and more hopeless and more unprofitable
than it had been before. In his own humble
way he had a monopoly of Ids own misery,
and he could never have fathomed the possi-
bilities of human degradation unless lie was
surrounded by envious competitors all eager
to steal Ids misery from Idm as the only
escape from a condition that was more
miserable still.
A Word with Mr. Gladstone.
“Tt is not given to great men to be great in all things.
Gladstone.
In point of far-reaching intellect, of varied scholar,
ship, of oratorical force, of ardent patriotism, of
unsullied life, Gladstone is great, if ever Eng-
]'simian was great. His very political somersaults
were possible only to a’person of supreme endow-
ments and magnetic force of character. Only a
man built of superfine metal could have stood the
test of that revolt against himself and his past
which he expressed in the declaration of his Home
Rule policy. It is possible that we Australians,
from considerations of distance, cannot accurately
gauge the greatness of that deed ; of the social and
political renunciations which it demanded ; of the
personal magnanimity of which it was a manifes-
tation. We have no criterion to measure it —or
him. Beside a Gladstone our politicians are as
mud-lumps moulded by childish hands contrasted
with the breathing sculptures of a Thorwaldsen
or a Power. Gladstone is great, or the word is
meaningless. And yet Gladstone is not great
all round and in all things; and it does not neces-
sarily follow that his words on every topic should
receive the respect due to an authority, though
they, should never fail to command the reverence
that is the prerogative of his fame. We feel called
upon to make this remark because, in bis latest
cabled utterance on Freetrade, he has taken up a
position which may be regarded as authoritative
simply because it is Gladstone’s.
As Chancellor of the Exchequer, Mr. Glad-
stone nui.de the most brilliant Budget speech on
record, and yet a later generation has not lies)
tated to pronounce his scheme of taxation more
ingenious than just. As a Poelite, he was a main
instrument in winning over the British people to the
< lobdenitccreed, and yet he has lived to witness the
Ministerial introduction into the House of Com-
mons of a measure of undeniably Protectionist
tendency, the Sugar Bounties Convention Bib,
and to confess to the Cobden Club itself that Free-
trade was fast losing its vitality as a practical
principle. As an expounder of the relation of
Freetrade to economic science, he lias had to sub
mif to have his contention that the theory is of
universal application conclusively controverted,
with respect to new countries, by •)', G. Blaine.
In short, Mr. Gladstone: has demonstrated that
in the department of economies he has mneli to
learn from smaller men. His political economy is
derived rather from the text-books than formed by
induction from the observation of industrial and
commercial facts. The data upon which he
founded Ins conclusions are of English origin, and
of the years embraced in the period between the
beginning of the Corn Law repeal movement and
the consummation of the French treaty by Cobden.
He has overlooked other conditions. In whatever
other fields of constructive statesmanship he has
triumphed, it must be said that tested by visible
results he has not built up an enduring reputation
in that of theoretical or applied economics. In
whatever other direction he has advanced, here he
has remained stationary. For instance, Protec-
tion appears to him to-day the hideous thing that
it did in 1846, when he regarded it as the cause of
a national suffering unparalleled in British history.
Men untrammelled by a traditional belief can now
discern in the distress of that time a product of
which a vicious system of landlordism was the
major factor and a crude fiscal policy the minor.
A novice in Liberalism, and still partially hampered
by the fetters of caste and of reverence for the
landed interests then inbred in every educated
Parliamentarian, it is not surprising that Mr.
Gladstone, 44 years ago, hesitated to put the
blame for the miserable condition of things
on to land-tenures. The view that he then
adopted has since become the conventional and
popular one. Till within the last year or two
nothing has occurred to challenge it, or otherwise
it is conceivable that the terms on which he holds
it may have been modified, just as he modified his
adherence to the popular estimate of Irish claims
when once circumstances compelled him to examine
the historical validity of the English position
respecting them. Great man as Mr. Gladstone
is it cannot be asserted of him that he is a great
economist.
, * # *
When, therefore, Mr. Gladstone, as cabled last
week, rebuked America and the majority of the
Australasian colonies for their adoption of Protec-
tive policies, and especially denounced as vicious in
principle arid as unjust as a practical measure the
exclusion of the Chinese, it follows that there is
no peculiar authority attaching to his words. He
repeated the shibboleths of his fiscal faith, that is
all; and of a faith, moreover, of which he is not
even a high-priest. He talked pure Cobdenism —
as was, of course, only meet and proper at a meet-
ing of the Cobden Club. And this is simply say-
ing that he spoke entirely from the standpoint of
British mercantilism and pawnbrokingism, and
in pure ignorance of the conditions under which
the Australian must struggle into industrial inde-
pendence and national life. So small, indeed, is
the acquaintance Mr. Gladstone has with the
position of affairs in Australia that he eulogised
N. S. Wales for her persistence in Freetrade, and
then in the next breath denounced the exclusion
of the Chinese as rigid Protection. In the first
place, as wo need not remind onr readers, N.S.
Wales does not persist in Freetrade: she
simply dallies with it. as her tariff includes
numerous duties avowedly imposed for protective
purposes which her alleged Freetrade Ministers
dare not repeal. In the second place, she has
upon her statute-book the most severely restric-
tive of all the Australian anti-Chinese Acts,
which measure was drafted by a Cobden Club
essayist, and introduced into Parliament by a
politician amongst whose questionable honours is
included honorary membership in that same in-
stitution. Mr. Gladstone could not have known
these things, or otherwise it is possible he would
have refrained from knocking the logical stuffing
out of Premier Pakkks and B. R. Wise, and from
politically reducing that other Cobden Clubite,
the Only Pulskohd. to a paralytic shadow.
* # *
Mr. Gladstone, pursuing his book theories to
their logical termination, cannot see anything in
anti-Chinese legislation but Protection. Parkeh
and Wise and Pulskohd and Alfred Allen and
“B. IV’ Caulfield, and other representative
Foreign-traders, deny there is any Protection in
it. Their denial impales them on a logical diffi-
culty, but they are so used to that process that it
does not hurt them in the least. The difference
between the respective conclusions of the Dis-
tinguished English Freetrader and the Undis-
tinguished Australian Freetraders is that the
former obeys logic and does not know the facts,
and that the latter know the facts and disobey
logic. The former is consistent with his creed:
the latter are the reverse. They may delude
themselves with the idea that they exclude the
Heathen Chinee solely because of the non-
assimilable character of his race, but in their
hearts they, as Gladstone puts it, “fear his
virtues rather than his vices’’ -they know him
to be, an economic problem as well as a mal-
odorous social difficulty. And they know, too,
that the existence of Australian Foreign-
traders as a political party would be
measured by hours and days were they
to go straight for pure Cobdenism —in other
words, if they consented to the unrestricted ad-
mission of “Chinese cheap labour.” With all
their bell-toppered disdain for the Australian
working man, and all their eagerness to buy their
labour in the cheapest market, the Foreign-traders
dare not go that length.
# * *
Now, Protectionists have never hidden either
from themselves or the community that in agitat-
ing for the exclusion of the Chinese they were
pursuing a policy of pure Protection. The ex-
clusion of the man who undersells onr labourer is
as integral an element in the Protectionist doc-
trine as the exclusion of the products of his labour,
and the Protectionist position on the question is
strengthened, and not weakened, when the cheaper
worker is of a race not only non-assimilable but
absolutely in all vital particulars antagonistic to
our own. We exclude the Chinese, as before long
we will have to exclude “our fellow-citizens under
the. Hag of the glorious British Empire,” the Sikh
and the Afghan, and all the rest of the Indian
niggers. Here is a paragraph from the Sydney
Morning H ehald of May Ift:
The Nerbndda, one of the British-India Company’s
steamers, is due here in a day or two from India and
the Straits Settlements. . . . Her report to hand
shows some :10 European passengers and no fewer than .>i
natives. The latter form a strange company. They com-
prise Sikhs. Pathans, and Afghans principally. The most


of them are traders in or hawkers'of Indian wares, and
have come to Australia, induced, it is said, by the glow-
ing- reports received from their countrymen. It has been
remarked that since the law was passed regulating the
immigration of the Chinese into certain parts of Australia
the Hindoo element has increased.
* # *
Now, these Hindoos, as we have been at some
pains to ascertain, land here under a contract
scarcely distinguishable from a deed of chattel-
slavery. Each man is paid by the importing firm
os. weekly and is “found’’ in addition —the total
per Vian U> lltr Importers uweroftiny twelve shit ■
linii;: mot jit i‘ Walt. Chinese “ artisan. .
II we may degrade, the term In using it, in this
connection, are splendidly paid when they receive
from.lss. to 20s. weekly, out of which they keep
themselves and save money. How is competition
between such people and the fair Australian pos-
sible? You ran only make it possible hy der/radine/
the more rosily worker to the level of the other.
This is the Protectionist reply. Mr. Glao-
sone, however, as a Freetrader, would answer
the question otherwise. “ Practise” he says, in
precisely so many words, “ the virtues of the
Asiatic', abstain from alcohol, for instance. ’ But
other abstinences besides that from brandy and
beer would be necessary to bring the Australian
to the economic level of the Asiatic. He would
have to abstain from meat and wheaten bread and
live on rice and pulse; he would have to dispense
with tweed and hide his nakedness with a waist-
cloth ; he would have to substitute a rag turban
for his felt hat; ho would have to cut Ids wife’s
and his children’s throats, for wives and offspring
are superfluous luxuries to the Asiatic as we know
him; and he would have to abstain from a borne-
life of even the poorest sort, and herd with a
dozen or twenty other “ abstainers ” in a style that
would shame a pig of decent breeding in some in-
sanitary shanty. He would have, further, to
abstain from dropping his hebdomadal threepence
into the collection-plate, for he would never go to
church ; he would have to spend his leisure in the
nameless vices of the Asiatic, because' he would
have no opportunity for an occasional visit to the
theatre or concert-room; he would no longer pur-
chase the penny-paper containing extracts from
Mr. (1 ladstone’ s review articles (contributor’s fee :
£24 per page), because he could not spare the
“bronze ’’for the purpose, or the time to read the
paper if he had it, inasmuch as to keep pace with his
Asiatic brother —and competitor—he would have
to work 18 hours per day, Sundays included. From
all these things would, sooner or later, the Aus-
tralian have to abstain, if ho would compete with
the Asiatic on the Asiatic—and Freetrade—level.
But, as the Australian neither can nor will live aw
the Asiatic does, and having, happily for himself,
the power to eliminate the degrading element from
the competition, lie chooses to exclude the Chinese.
And as he treats the Chinese, so will he treat the
Hindoo, whether or not England bices it. The
trifling fact that the latter is a eo-subject of what
Sir Henry Parkes calls “ The Empire,” will not
deter him from receiving similar respectful treat-
ment to that already administered to the Chow.
Because England lias seen fit to exploit India for
the monetary aggrandisement of her “classes” and
for the eternal salvation of the conquered people,
~ the reader will please place his tongue in his
cheek here is no reason why Australia should be
made an exploiting ground in her turn. If Hin-
doos and Cingalese continue to come here as they
have been doing, another Exclusion Act. will be
necessary. • And should the imperial authorities
object to our poll-taxing our brethren of the tawny
tint-well, Australia will not have much trouble
to find a pair of shears that will slit “the silken
cords.” People have not forgotten the half-black
compositors imported by the Empire authorities
with a view to curbing the arrogance of the cheeky
white man.
* # *
No, Australians won’t follow Mr. Gladstone
when his philosophic liberalism runs to seed, and
least of all when he becomes unconsciously the
organ of British Mercantilism. This system of
sanctimonious brigandage has its Siva and Bhow
anek, evil deities who must be propitiated by
sacrifices. It styles them Unrestricted Competi-
tion and Cheap Labour. To them it has offered
up the British artisan, and the Indian ryot and
mill-worker. To them it will consecrate the
African nigger when he has been sufficiently
civilised by “bullets and Bibles.” To them it
would throw the people of Australia—if they
could. But they cannot: at least there shall be
one spot on earth where British Mercantilism and
its gods shall be refused sway. The brutal frank-
ness of a Von Caprivi in his now historic phrase,
pictured the whole British system of commercialism
with minute faithfulness. It operates on a heathen
country by slaughtering —from beneath the shelter
of the Christian Cross ; by exploiting—under the
name of the commercial enterprise; by civilising—
with the refining agency of the rifle and the rum-
bottle. It cannot adopt these methods in deal-
ing with its own colonies, but the principle of its
conduct there is the same; it thrives in proportion
to the destruction of colonial enterprise and to the
sapping of colonial virility. To the, heathen, it
talks Bible and acts bullet; to the Australian and
Canadian colonist it preaches freedom of exchange
and trade, and acts so as to undermine internal
liberty. It cannot force the products of its cheap
labour into protected places, but it seeks to in-
troduce the labour itself. The exclusion of the
labour then becomes a vital necessity. A policy
to that end is Protection. Freetraders, as wo
have them in New South Wales, say it isn’t. Pro-
tectionists say it is, and here Mr. Gladstone and
the Protectionists agree. Which is the same
thing as though Mr. Gladstone were to call New
South Wales Freetraders sorry humbugs.
“The Sentimental Juryman."
Under this caption the Australasian, with
characteristic Tory callousness, lashes with its
strip of bullock-hide the jurymen in the United
Devonshire mine (Vie.) murder-case for their
“weak sentimentality” and their “sympathy
with crime’’—the wrath of the Melbourne paper
having been excited by the admittedly peculiar
reason for the qualification which accompanied
their verdict of guilty. “Guilty of murder, with
a recommendation to mercy because the prisoner
did not intend to murder the deceased,” is a find-
ing that would produce laughter at its illogicalness
were the circumstances less serious. Murder in
the eyes of the law implies wilfulness, and there-
fore felonious intent; otherwise, the act which takes
life is urn of manslaughter. Tkevethick’s mur-
derer did not intend tokillhis victim(anymorethan,
perhaps, did Morrison, of Sydney, the recently-
executed alleged burglar), but ho did purpose to
commit a certain felonious deed from the execu-
tion of which purpose the capital crime resulted.
Legally, therefore, the act which deprived TltKVß-
tiiick of life was murder. The jurymen couldn’t
evade the fact, but they thought they could avert
its legal consequences by appending a recommen-
dation which they must have known was, from a
legal point of view, ill-founded and irrational. To
this extent they were weak and sentimental. Why
did they put’ themselves into this quandary?
Simply becaufe they didn’t want the man to hang.
And so it will ever be while the punishment
of death remains on the statute-book. Of
course, The Australasian moralist—-a Draco
in petto— can’t see this; _ he only notices an
offended Justice and an insulted Society. He
won’t see that rather than deliver a criminal to
the hangman the average juryman who has before
him the alternatives of murder and perjury,
prefers the lesser crime, wriggles himself into the
most tortuonsly-illdgieal of .knots, and won’t con-
sent to lip straightened out if the process is to bt-
followed by I he Melon being slaughtered by the
( h a eiTimrn t's stl pend laps assassin. I he aseiage
juror feels that human nature, and particularly
the legal portion of it, is fallible, and he likes his
sleep o’ nights to be undisturbed by a recollection
that he has possibly been one of a dozen or so in-
struments which have sent a creature to an unal-
terable fate. While a man lives, _ though it
be in tomb-like monotony of a “silent” cell,
reparation is to some extent possible if reparation
be demanded; but once he has been slaughtered
reparation is a luxurious superfluity. The average
juror not being a Judge or (Jrown prosecutor--
i.r., not having “ a legal training,” is utterly
averse to the shedding of blood, and is when out-
side Court and remote from thc_ over-aweing and
unaccustomed influences of judicial wigs and lock-
ings-up-for-the-night, prepared to see the whole
race of logicians d —d first before he 11 make one
of the signatories to a dea th-warrant. Ho wouldn’t
mind consigning a man to gaol for “life,” in fact,
he’d be rather pleased at the idea, for the average
juror has no great horror of gaol for others. But
give the hangman a job he will not if he can
possibly help it. Rather than run the risk of hav-
ing a pricked conscience, he will chance the
letting loose on society of an assassin. Remove,
however, from before his eyes the vision _ of
a murder in which he has a share, by abolishing
the death-penalty ; or if you refuse to do that,
give him the right to legally differentiate as to
the degree of the criminal’s heinousness ; and then
see what he will do. He won’t put the judge out
of temper and make the C.P. look black by a
leaning to leniency then. Not a bit of it. If be
errs at all it is the prisoner who will be affected
prejudicially and not institutional justice ; for as
his own personal mental comfort will not be dis-
turbed in an unbearable degree by any suffering
of the criminal’s short of death, he will look upon
himself as a functionary of the law who, ipso facto,
must be against the prisoner. Make the hang-
man's hemp as obsolete as the virgin’s boot, the
rack, or the scavenger’s daughter; or limit the
discretionary power of the judge by increasing
that of the jury, and yon will save the stony-
hearted Tory journalist the trouble of inveighing
against sentiment in the jury-box. Australian
juries don’t merely want to have the privilege of
saying whether a prisoner is guilty or not g'uilty ;
they also wish to say, in the event of their finding
him culpable, hoio guilty he is—whether, for
example, he has committed murder in the first,
second or third degree. And this privilege they
will secui’e long ere the youngest of the Supreme
Court judges now sitting retires on a fat pension.

In a leader, dated May 15, Sydney Daily Tele-
graph asserted that the speech of Mr. I firms,
leader of the opposition, on the federation question
was ‘ ‘ from first to last in the region of ambiguities. ’
To confute this statement it will only be necessary
to quote Mr. Hums' peroration ;
“ Our people are quite competent to defend these shores
against the whole Chinese nation. I have stated as
clearly as language can put it that I am in favour of
federation, but I do not think that the proposal of the
Premier can ever be successful. I believe in another form
of federation, which may he nearer at hand than we
think, by which our Australian colonies shall he one
nation. It is better for ns to remain a dependency of
England than to forfeit such independence as we now
possess for such an alliance as Sir Henry Parkks proposes.
I will give to any proposal to bring about federation on
proper lines my strongest possible support. This colony
may be the birth-place of a nation, or it may not, but it
is the hope of all our people to see the flag of the Southern
Cross flying, not only over the whole of Australasia, but
over Australasia as a united nation.
It will be seen that the speaker, like many true
Australians, has no desire to saddle the country
with Governors-General, a whole troop of British
sinecure-hunters, and “Upper Houses” of the
British peers brand, but prefers to await the
federation that will come about with a pacific
separation from the mother-country and will be
instituted under Republican rules. The Bulletin
welcomes Mr. Dibbs to the Republican camp—
and hopes he doesn’t come there out of pure
cussedness.
Editor Bulletin, —“ Let this day be a warning to
all tyrants,” said Thomas Nixon, the poor
hunted son of an evicted Irish farmer as he read
the Declaration of American Independence in
Philadelphia over a century ago. “ Let this be a
warning,” said Governor Hopetoun the other day
to the students of Melbourne University as he
proclaimed aloud the corruptions and factions of
American Republics. Since the arrival of the
present “My Lord,” Governor to Victoria, some
of the citizens have gone frantic with loyalty to
the monarchial institution invented by savages for
savages, and when we read in the daily press that
“ Lord ” Hopetoun is about to visit some place,
Radicals ask, like Victor Hugo: “The people
call a man with a large estate a Lord. When they
pray to the Supreme Being they address Him as
Lord. Each has the same equal title. Which of
them Is God ?” Members of the Upper House in
Victoria, mayors of townships, leaders of trades-
processions, Orange Lodges and Archbishop Carr
ought to answer. All hail the Australian Re-
public ! with a grand charter of human liberty
beaming down upon the sons and daughters of
men. A Republic, the rule of the common people,
wherein Liberty shall build up her noble fanes
based upon Justice, Reason and Peace. Such a
Constitution will ever flourish while monarchy and
its hosts of long-forgotten tyrants and their
parasites shall be “ dishonoured and in the pit.”—
Spirit op the Woods.

Many of them are absolute novices in the

of salaried duty necessary in a magistrate
who has to consider charges against old friends
and acquaintances. Nobody doubts that. Mr.
Panton, despite the snobbish Bkkrthirst inci-
dent. will do justice to the Law but what is one
Panton among SO many irresponsible casual beaks,
each having an equal voice with himself in a
decision? The real hearing of these fraud sum-
menses will commence on the 27th, and if 27
•honorary Holomons roll up to the Bench, as they

There must be something in living constantly in
an atmosphere of stale beer which develops a sort
of mental strabismus among most publicans
sooner or later. How, otherwise, can the follow-
ing deliverances by Air. Header, President of the
Victorian Licensed Victuallers’ Association, be
accounted for ?
He asked, was not the evidence of an hotelkeeper as
good as that of any other man ? Here was a policeman
(referring to the ease of a publican who had lost Ids
licence for Sunday-trading) who, beyond the fact of bis
being a policeman, had no stake in the colony, pitted
against men who have from £5OO to £5OOO, and more, to
consider, and yet the publican’s word goes for nought.
The affliction we have alluded to prevents Air.
Header from seeing that it is just beetnint tin-
publicans have so much to lose that their word
goes for nought. Their love of truth in the ab-
stract is hardly likely to cause them to lose the
license of a concrete corner pub. Air. Header
added, referring to working-men, and this, more
than the other, make us think he must have been
slightly deroosted: “If the publicans went
out on strike, and refused to supply drink, how
would these men look ?” Dry, no doubt, for a time.
But they would get over it. If these haughty swipe-
sellers were to spontaneously combust as one man
to-morrow, the country would sober up and think
it was well rid of them, in a week, and then the
country would gradually construct another set of
publicans. It is a mistake for them to fancy the
visible universe is run in the interests of Beer. 11
is a mistake also to take it for granted that the
working-man regards them in the light of bene-
factors. The working-man isn’t always drunk.

Alr, Reid, President of the Melbourne Chamber
of Commerce, has incited a local journalist ic get
out of his depth and flounder helplessly in the
troubled waters of political economy. Air. Reid
was guilty of repeating the honourable chestnut
that “ all wealth comes from mother earth,” to
which the misguided critic replies that “all wealth,
or nearly all, comes from labour, and mother
earth, in the sense of mere production of raw
material, is but a very small contributor either to
accumulated wealth or the necessities of man.”
This is not very lucid, but lucid enough to show
that the writer is fairly off his legs, and had
better struggle back to the shallows of yellow-
backed lore. Raw material—in which must be
included raw fish —constitutes the entire wealth
of the world, and directly material pro-
duced by labour from earth or sea, or
lying ready to hand, is operated upon by
the most primitive form of handicraft, then
it ceases to be raw material. Reid was right, and
the other fellow makes the grand old mistake of
thinking that money is wealth in the true sense of
the term. Earth and water supply the where-
withal of every manufactured article, and the
means to support the labour which is manufactur-
ing it. Labour makes the money-value of natural
wealth, but that is all; and if every coin in the
world were melted down into one great lump
to-morrow, the world wouldn’t lose an iota of
wealth, although the inhabitants might die of in-
convenience. The Melbourne journalist evidently
thinks that the man who buys a fish for three-
pence and sells it for sixpence after frying it, has
added something to the wealth of nations.
The “Daily Telegraph” and Its Editors.
With reference to the article which appeared in
our issue of the 10th instant regarding the resig-
nation of Mr. F. W. Ward as editor of the Sydney
Daily Telegraph, we have carefully investigated
the matter at issue, and are satisfied that the pro
posed sale of the Sydney tramways was not, on
the part of the Board of Directors, the subject of
disagreement.
The directors held that it was their right to
decide the broad policy of the paper, whilst the
editor claimed the privilege of determining every-
thing upon his own judgment. As Mr. Ward
was in ill-health and had applied for extended
leave of absence with the view of visiting San
Francisco, it was decided to set the matter at rest
before his locum tcnens was installed, and accord-
ingly a resolution was passed providing that all new
questions of broad and important State policy
should be determined by the Directors in consulta-
tion with the responsible Editor. Mr. Ward de-
clined to accept this resolution, and thereupon
tendered his resignation.
We have to express our regret that in our
article on the subject anything should have ap-
peared capable of being regarded as an imputation
on the Directorate.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, May 21,1890.
Mv Dbar Moorabinda, —
Quite the feature-to me, at least—of the present
N.S.W. Parliament is the tabling of the Female Suffrage
Bill—and hardly anything else is talked of for the time
being. The thing may be good or bad, hut I notice that
its chief advocates up to now are those women whose
narrow sectarian environment causes them to look upon
the stage as an “ unclean thing,” hut who yet long
thirstily for the footlights and the faces beyond and so
seize on any proposal for “ reform” so that they may pose
on platforms and torment audiences with the rasp of their
“ cock-sure ” convictions. The same spirit that moves a
girl with pretty ankles to join an opera-company actuates
the large - waisted, vehement she - politician to hurl
anathema at man and all his works. Both are determined
to display their natural gifts to admiring crowds, and
both found the domestic tie unbearable. I hope the Bill
is thrown out—but, if it should happen to go through,
just let them insert a clause conferring the suffrage only
on women over 30 years of age. They won’t need to en-
large the electoral-roll then—at least, not so very much.
Of course the dailies are full of wild letters on the suf-
frage question, and one enthusiast considers it trium-
phantly in favour of the female vote that “ women ate in
the majority in our churches.” Of course, and for exactly
the same reason that men are in the majority at leg-
shows. But if instead of the unctuous man of Trewth a
charming lady D.D. arose in the rostrum and lisped the
Sunday service, you would find a cut-away coat and hard-
hat congregation from the door to the altar-rails. Women
so often go to church to “ sit under” some “ dear man”—
the mashing element being very strong in sacred estab-
lishments, probably because it can go to any lengths un-
questioned. For instance, a leading Australian divine in-
vited a dear sister to take a run up the Mountains with
him the other day, and she went and returned while a
censorious world wagged on as usual.
In an article “On the Block” in the S.M. Herald, the
writer makes a Johnny Crapaud, whom he is towing
round, say: “ I love not once, but often, to mingle with a
well-bred, well-dressed, happy throng.” Now, I have
stood this sort of thing long enough ; hut to have that
dingy promenade described as a resort for “ well-bred,
well-dressed people,” is the very last camel. In truth,
very few girls who move in society do it at all—the
suburban girls whose joys are few—schoolgirls and girls
who ought to be, and the Chosen are its most faithful ad-
herents—with a sprinkling of hobbledehoys, young men,
and the battalion of used-up, shady men-about-town who
are as invariable as the post-office clock and equally
reliable. Of course you may see anyone on the Block,
since it is a business thoroughfare, hut the inveterate
“doers” of it are the social olla-podrida mentioned
above. And if you don’t think so, “ do ” it yourself and
see.
Rosehill was not exactly en fete for the pony-races on
14th inst., which somehow don’t thrill one like horse-
racing does. There were no gowns and no band, but the
course looked lovely ; the trees have grown fast and the
flowers that bloom in the autumn tra-la are doing very
well in the parterres ; also, the sight of a waiter in full
rig lends additional attraction to the latticed tea-room.
Besides which, the secretary has the same winning way of
wearing his hat, and the trains are surprisingly punctual.
The two concluding days of the intercolonial lawn-
tennis tournament, which has attracted sassiety audiences
from the first, were remarkable for the number of soulful
frockeries displayed on the soggy turf. On 13th perhaps
the gown with most “go” about it was a leaf-yellow
woollen overskirt with draperies designed in crushed
strawberry over a jupe of crushed strawberry silk made
plainly with a kilting at the feet, bodice florally draped
with cream chiffon front, capote of mignonette and pink
ribbons. Another nice attire was a moonshine-green
Russell cord made plainly with bands of green velvet at
the feet and on the bodice, bonnet in green with lilies of
the valley. A workmanlike gown in blue and green tartan
topped by a flapdoodle shapo was a good fit even if the
wearer didn’t look like a skittle; and Brother Rupert
stood around in a degage way in a bright blue tie. By
the way, Gov. Carington wears bright blue ties and tan
hoots, with a slim toothpick in his rosebud mouthlet.
On 14th nisi, hostilities ceased, and Lady Carington
handed the gorgeous prizes (which your Sappho sincerely
hopes won’t turn out to he plated) to the proud, pumped
winners, who looked about as untidy as one would wish to
see. The lady players especially seemed not to wot of (he
great moral effect a clean face has on the nasty public,
and the vistas I got (of course there's no harm in my
saying it) of clocked hose in strange hues unprocurable in
the city marts, points the moral that if cleanliness is next
to what’s-’is-name, lawn tennis is very close to the pepper.
There was quite a throng on the decisive day, and of
course the championship skipped over the border, and I
do hope the Cup winner will drink out of it en route,
and so he enabled to whisk ii past (he Alhury Hora-
tius as a “ toilette-necessary. Lady Carington—who
presented the prizes from a railed and carpeted space
on the lawn—wotc fawn woollen with guipure braid
over a r \n velvet skirt, folded front of rose pink chiffon,
with stn» bonnet adorned with folds of fawn velvet and
wings. rs. Acland Hood rode (with Captain Acland
Hood and brother Rupert) sporting a baggy covert coat
over a crimson vest, with a straw sailor hat on top—a
mixed-up suit that suggested the cruel idea that she
didn’t pack her habit this trip. I can’t chronicle all the
sweet things in furbelows I was surrounded with, since a
girl with bronze hair and eyes in a sheath of rust-brown
oloth and a crimson rose in her brown velvet hat took all
the attention I had to spare from the weird and compli-
cated wriggling of the prize-winners, who mostly seemed
to feel their position keenly. Due girl I especially rccol
leot executing a reverse-action duck-curtsey with a rose
pink kerosene-lamp in either hand.
Regarded as a frock-show, the Carington ball on 14th
inst. at Sydney Exhibition, was a very successful festivity,
but looked upon from a dancing standpoint one dips one’s
finger in dust to write its epitaph. Spying about the room
it really seemed all women and, owing to the fact that we
were literally six to one, many a nice girl languished discon-
tentedly with her train crumpled up under her and no
chance to peacock even for a promenade. The naval men,
who were there in great force and rig, did double duty,
certainly, and it was a wonder the Baron did not
come to the rescue, instead of sitting as gracefully as he
knows how on the dais, surrounded by rows of seated
loveliness, brandishing blank programmes. But some of
the gowning was really breathless. Lady Carington
looked like a pretty airy fairy in bodice and train of pure
white brocade, over a jupe of white gauze striped with
silver, with clusters and trails of pale-pink roses and
leaves, and diamonds sparkling everywhere, from her
tiara to her white satin shoes. Mrs. Aciand-Hood wore
electric-blue, with grey lights about it, mingled with soft
greyish crepe. Lady Charles Scott affected “yallev, a
colour which Mrs. Burdekin also appeared in, the simple
silken gown made without a train and relieved with black
velvet bows on the shoulders and right hip, bands of black
velvet inside the corsage, diamond necklace ; Miss
Walenn flesh-pink silk ; Lilian Seccombe, black lace and
moire sash ; Mrs. Andrews, stylish black gown with front
composed of frills of silk chenille fringe relieved with
clusters of myrtle. A lovely attire worn by Miss Marks
was in pale sulphur-yellow silk, the bodice adorned with
folds of chiffon and gold lace, plain skirt designed from
the feet in gold fern pattern ; harlequin points of chiffon
edged with three frosted gold balls which must have re-
vived most unpleasant memories in many present.
Another nice vision was a white silk with panels of white
tulle, and dotted about on corsage and skirt with tiny
bouquets of velvet leaves in autumn shades—but she
shouldn’t have carried a pink fan Mrs. D. C. Bell wore
soft pink satin brocade, en traine, over a skirt of brocade
and lace in stripes, bodice decorated with unmounted
roses in pink and cream, pink plume fan. Another hand-
some pink gown, worn by Mrs. Payne, was in pale dawn
pink with silvery pattern brocade forming the bodice
and train and panels, over a skirt of tinted tulle
with lines of soft pink roses, panels edged with pearl pas-
sementerie bodice adorned with unmounted roses.
There ! are not these mouth-watery ? And I could go on
for an hour with others equally tasteful, yet there are one
or two little things your Sappho wants to point her finger
at. One was a large stout lady gotten up A la Hamlet’s
mother in black velvet and stagey yellow satin puffs and
slashings with a gilt crown with a feather in it adjusted
rakishly on a howlingly wiggy wig. Another was a long
“might-have-been” sort of charmer, who had trimmed
herself sadly in autumn leaves. Indeed, numbers had
autumn-leaf trimming and bouquets, and considering the
question quite calmly, I think these sere relievements are
generally too cruelly en suite for society wear. For the
rest the floor was easy, the music eccentric, the supper
complete, and the decorations beautiful, though why they
didn’t have rendezvous, anil so save a lot of worry, isn t
apparent up to date.
The last night of “David Garrick'’ at Sydney Royal on
16th inst. was remarkable for the dense throng of
humanity all over the house, things being S.R.O. before
the orchestra had tuned up-a state of affairs that the
intrinsic merits of “David" didn’t exactly merit. Of
course Bellew was the star of the evening in white satin
breeches and a severe cold, but Mi’s. Potter, who had
only to look nice and swoon gracefully when the dramatist
got stuck for a word, did not appeal, and even her ward-
robe failed as a source of solace. In act one she wore a
clinging robe in soft white stuff with long sash of pale-
blue and a pelerine with long ends at the hack in black
gauze and lace; hair touzled, of course— into a few of the
wildest of tails held in by a pale-blue hand. In the second
part of act two, when she changes her gown for the sub-
jugation of Garrick, the toilette becomes more interesting.
The bodice and train are in pale iron-grey brocade with,
at the right side, a panel in dawn-grey satin brocade lined
witli primrose, the jnpc of soft white material fall-
ing in soft, plain lines; bodice edged with a deep
frill of white laoc with long, puffy sleeves in the
white; belt and long bow-and-ends hanging at the
front of primrose ribbon, grey shoes, white gloves, a
screen-fan in white feathers, and a yellow bow crowning
her mussed-up coiffure. In the last act the frock is dove-
grey just touching the floor, with tops of sleeves in grey
velvet, with V-shaped folds in soft white material forming
the front of the bodice ; archamlogically correct black
cape, which made the sylph-like artiste look all angles,
and a big black hat which had a bad habit of going off at
a ridiculous tangent in moments of reciprocated passion.
There were several pretty gowns in front. Frances
Saville wore a dreamy confection in ivory satin and lace,
with a few dewy-looking diamonds about it. A brown
spotted net, with full shoulder-puffs and a garnet neck-
lace, looked uncommon and pretty. There were several
theatre-parties in the house—the largest being lit up by
the presence of Captains French and Helbert, who had
got off the vice-regal chain for once in a way.
Too big a crowd for the badly-ventilated “ great Hall ’’
assembled at Sydney University on 13th inst. to hearken
to U.M. Society’s rendering of Cowen’s “ Rose Maiden.’>
The platform was tastefully gotten up with swaying reeds
and pot-plants, and all the choristers wore roses with
pretty effect. Mrs. Macdonald, who sang divinely, wore
flesh-pink and white with corsage bouquet of roses,
though why she added a bright blue aigrette in her golden
locks isn’t evident ; while Miss Naylor wore ivory silk en
traine relieved with black velvet. The chorus-singing
was very powerful and decidedly church-choirifled, hut
they have a silvery tenor up there, who, if he sang with
less anxiety, would he as sweet as a had in a tree, which,
is more than can lie said of the leading tenor, who was com-
pletely flabbergasted from the start, and sang horribly
flat whenever things became critical. By the bye, I was
surprised to see the initials “ V.M.S.” inscribed on a
groggy blue banner “ Urns,” Is punctuation obsolete at
the ’Varsity '!
The revival of tliat dismal Jimmy", “Two Orphans,” at.
Her Majesty’s on 17th inst. was attended by a dotted d.c.
and a crowd everywhere else, Olympia being especially
full and vociferous. In this lachrymose stage narrative
the men have all the dressing, Jewett wearing pale-blue
satin and lashings of silver lace as if he ate his dinner in
it; while Sweeney, in primrose satin with a pink flower,
looked like a superior china ornament given in exchange
for old do’. Indeed the rage for purple and line linen led
the management to clothe the sob-strangled Pierre in
stag-brown velveteen of the newest description, which
looked rather lugubrious when contrasted with Mamma
Fouchard, who was apparently robed in old dish-cloths.
There was no dressing in the d.c., though a red cashmere
with neck cut star-shape, bonnet with red velvet and filled
in with red gauze joining a high collar of velvet-full
sleeves to the elbow caught in with a band of velvet,
looked festive and sensible. After all, what is the use of
donning deeollettee and a pink rose during the winter
when theatres are very cold comfort and you have to sit
bundled up in plush and fur like an Esquimaux or risk
the neuralgia?
I have received from Paling and Co. a copy of “ La
Favorite ” valse written by Eille Norwood and dedicated
in Mrs. Brough, a sepia tinted presentment of whom
adorns the title-page. Now, when Mr. Norwood is acting
he isn’t very, very good, but when he gets entangled in
three-four time he is ’orrid. The as it were,
of “La Favorite” valse is reminiscent of a demented
daddy-longlegs learning the Highland fling, and when the
composer can’t think of a chord for the bass he simply
scores the same octaves tor both hands, and the result is
jerky. The valse, however, has one great merit—it is
very short.
I was glad to see that the fourth Menk-Meyer recital on
15th Inst, was hearkened to by- a full house which keenly
appreciated the melody, but, as for dressing, there was
the same “ penny plain” on one chair and “ twopence-
colourcd” on the next, which seems always tlie rule in
the depressing atmosphere of the Y.M.C.A. The pro-
gramme was, as usual, comprehensive, commencing with
Wagner and ending with a valse, the “ Tannhauser March,’
which was vociferously encored, while the fact that
Rubinstein’s “ Toreador en Espagnole,” a gay and giddy
“ piece,” made people smile, then laugh outright, proves
how thoroughly the spirit of the thing was interpreted by
Miss Menk-Meyer, who was the recipient of much ttorr.l
compliments, and looked charming in cream silk and lace
relieved with gold passementerie.
Sydney Suburban Bicycle Club sports on the Association
ground on 17th inst. was a soppy, sloppy sorrow which
seemed too limp to get lively even when the sun shone.
The course was churned mud, and the competitors must
have cleaned their wheels next day with gags on to pre-
vent the heat of their remarks setting fire to their hair.
Under the damp circumstances I suppose I shouldn’t
criticise the performers’ attire, hut I can’t help mention-
ing a youth with a Percy Lyndal profile, a pink shirt and
a dirty dog’s-eared collar, who evidently didn’t believe in
wasting clean linen in a Scotch mist. The dressing on the
lawn was of the “ won’t spoil” order, though some of the
complexions would have been wrecked by a drop of rain.
A pretty dollish girl wore sage-green Russell cord with
sage-green velvet sleeves, and a little terrier in a big,
black muff. Lots of men said they longed to be that
terrier, and they got offended when someone told them
not to fret, but to hurry along and offer themselves as the
muffs.
Speaking of music, Ann’s Montague, who is stopping
at the Hotel Metropole, and is engaged to support the
Halids during their Sydney season at the Centenary Hall,
York-street (when Miss Naylor will he the accompanist),
received a pretty anonymous compliment in the shape of
a colossal and beautiful wreath of white flowers an hour
after she landed in Sydney. No card was attached, and
whether it was the combined effort of a society or not
hasn't been discovered so far.
That ancient proverb about drowning the dog of ill
repute fits in well with regard to hospital-nurses, who are
not all convention ally-attired Betsy Prigs. Nobody is so
illiberal as to believe that there are not generous self-
sacrificing natures shrouded in the nurse’s cloak and veil,
but that there arc also quite reverse attributes under the
same prim gowning goes without saying. These piebald
nursings are induced by the recent remarks of an ex-nurse
to whom I talked the other day. Said she: “Just when
you think somebody will die before to-morrow and you
make an appointment on the chance they always hang on
to spoil you. Do you know what I used to do ? If they
were really given up I used to stop the nourishment once
or twice—after all it was kinder to. shorten their suffer-
ings !” T know I shall get into trouble over this, but it is
a solemn fact all the same.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
George says that Huxley goes to Nature
for his science and to the Duke of Argyll for his
economics.
Some members of the N.S.W. Assembly
persist in smoking in the library. Your ordinary
smoker is as selfish as a pretty woman.
The Bulletin much regrets the cab-
accident by which Premier Parkes’ leg was
broken. As the fracture is a compound one its
ultimate issue may be more serious than the tough
old man anticipates, and will probably, for a con-
siderable time, pospone Ids ambitious projects.
However, it may safely be wagered that the
veteran—whose ease, by the way, is in the hands
of Dr. Maurice O’Connor —will re-appear in Par-
liament at no far distant date.
According to the report presented at the
forty-first annual meeting of the Australian
Mutual Provident Society their business is largely
in excess of that of the preceding year, and is,
with two exceptions, the largest year’s business
yet transacted. Claims have been paid on the
deaths of 040 members, amounting to h0''0,025,
and the accumulated fund, despite those deduc-
tions, now amounts to the noble sum of £8,011,020!
A meeting will be held in August for the purpose
of amending bye-laws.
“ Mr. Phil. May, a young artist who spent
some years in Sydney.” The Social Gossip letter
in Saturday’s Argus mentions that Phil, has pro-
duced “ a superb piece of draftmanship and por-
traiture” for St. Stephen’s Review and then
goes through a mean pretence of explaining that
he is a young artist who spent some years in
Sydney. The man who wrote that letter, and the
editor who passed or altered tin; reference to Phil.
May knew perfectly well that he came out under
engagement to The Bulletin, that his name is a
household world wherever The Bulletin goes,
which is all over Australasia, and that, moreover,
he is still under engagement to the paper.
The W.A. Bulletin, a few-weeks’-old
print, which calmly and even in a godfathcrly
way refers to this paper as its “ namesake,” pub-
lishes a long string of unacknowledged clippings,
prose and verse, from The Bulletin. The column
is headed with an inkstand and entitled “ Ink-
-Bplashes.” “ Paste splashes,” ’dear brother,
would sound ever so much better. Which reminds
us that a certain Sydney paper is now printing as
original a lot of stories cribbed from the Yankee,
but badly and unblushingly localised. The
Bulletin will take a very early opportunity of
pointing out where these enterprising people, who
never spend any money on hiring clever pens, but
only on leasing big shears, get their matter from.
While on this subject, we may as well tell an in-
structive yarn which we heard the other day. One
of Australia’s best all-round journalists—a man
whose compeers may be counted on the fingers of a
hand and a-half, lately took a story to the editor
of a rich N.S.W. weekly. “Itis a pity,” senten-
tiously remarked the editor, “ that one can get
from local writers hardly a line of well-polished
Australian fiction. To procure such matter one is
forced to go to England. Some of those who
write in England tales, the plots whereof are laid
in Australia, have, it is true, but the smallest per-
sonal knowledge of this country--yet, how clean-
cut is their work ! Jam sorry to have to decline
your story.” The writer sadly pocketed his MS.
and departed. He gave the tale another name
and sent it to England—and had it accepted by a
magazine. From that magazine it was clipped by
the N.S.W. editor who had declined it in manu-
script, and who after all duly published it in his
paper on the clr ap.

SERIOUS ACCIDENT TO MR. HENNIKER
HEATON.
London, Tuesday. .
A serious accident occurred to-day to Mr. Hen-
niker Heaton, M.P. for Canterbury. As he was
passing the Nelson Column in Trafalgar-square,
where some workmen were repairing the figure at
the top, a large piece of masonry fell on to his
head. The stone was shattered into a thousand
pieces, and it is expected that the honourable.
member will be detained in his room for a few days.

THE COLONIAL CRICKETERS.
AUSTRALIA v. ALL-ENGLAND.
SPLENDID STAND BY THE COLONIALS.
London, Tuesday.
It is estimated that at least 80,000 assembled at
Kennington Oval to-day to witness the commence-
ment of the match between the Australians and
the All-England Eleven. Tire Englishmen won
the toss, but could make no stand against the
deadly bowling of Turner and Ferris, and were all
disposed of for the paltry score of 584. The field-
ing of the colonials was much admired, there being
only 40 extras. Dr. Barrett and Murdoch opened
for the visitors. ' determined stand was made,
the score being 11 when Murdoch was caught.
Gregory was bowled first ball. His defence was
much admired, and he was loudly applauded on
returning to the pavilion. Much dissatisfaction
was expressed when Blackball] was given out for
knocking his wicket clean out of the ground, and
breaking the wicket-keeper’s leg while attempting
to cut a ball from Lohmann. The umpire’s
decision was hooted. Ferris joined Barrett, but
soon retired, giving a Ferris simple catch to point
which was accepted. The Englishmen’s fielding
w'as very loose at times. At the conclusion of the
day’s play, the score stood at 87 for (5 wickets,
Barrett being not Out 2, an invaluable contribu-
tion to his side. The match is regarded as a sure
victory for the colonials.

A SENSATIONAL DIVORCE.
London, Thursday.
A rumour is being freely circulated amongst the
London clubs that a sensational divorce-case will
shortly occupy the attention of the Courts. The
petitioner is the proprietress of a whelk-stall in
the Borough-road, and the names of a well-known
undertaker, two policemen, an octogenarian
bishop, and a notorious demi iiionduirir arc men-
tioned. Some startling evidence is expected.

The “ christening” advertisement humbug
is spreading in Sydney. We see now chronicled
in the daily papers the bygone baptisms of whole
families, with their “Ivanhoe Fitzaltamonts ”
and their “ Rosalie Blanche Violets.”
A N.S.W. book-agent, having been re-
pulsed from the residence of a Clarence River
farmer with sundry agricultural implements such

That somewhat incoherent, but highly
dramatic, story of the “Two Orphans” lias been
well-staged at’Sydney Her Majesty’s. The stage
arrival of a four-horsed diligence from Normandy
with the lachrymose sisters is a good example of
that straining after realism which threatens in
time to reduce stage-pictures to a mere photo-
graphic delineation in which so much is clearly
outlined that little can be suggested. This ten-
dency towards naturalism will inevitably lessen
the circle of stage-admirers, for the skilful sug-
gestions of a genius strike a responsive chord in
natures the most dissimilar, while a clearly-defined
situation might attract one and repel another.
William Rignold plays the burly, sensual idler,
Jacques, with picturesque ease and wonderful
agility. In his praise we need only say that the
performance is quite up to his Jack Saxton
(he has not yet quite got rid of the York-
shire accent), but as he first made the acquaint-
ance _of a Sydney audience in that character
it will probably continue to be the one with
which they will always most closely associate
him. The Pierre of A. E. Greenaway is such a
monotonous-toned person that one has little sym-
pathy with his misfortunes, though in the last act
he almost got away from his tendency to preach.
As Armand, Henry J ewett plays a small part effec-
tively. We have had several stage-duels in Sydney
recently, but his rapier encounter with the Mar-
quis dePresies( Henry Hoyte) is by a long way the
best managed. Roland Watts-Phillips is a capable
Henrietta; but as Louise, with her true and
sympathetic singing voice, Bessie Rignold ex-
ceeded most folks’ expectations. As the abbess
who does evil that good may come, Mrs. Rignold
scores heavily, and the Frochard of Maggie Oliver
is a good performance, though it would bear the
infusion of a little more devilry. Maribel Green-
wood as Marianne achieves her best up to date,
and Bessie Colville is much more endurable than
in “Now-a-Days.”
*# * *
The representation, at Sydney Royal, of
Mnskerry s adaptation, from the German, we
believe, of the hypothetical story of an actor whose
drunkenness was assumed and not real, afforded
Kyrle Bellew, as David Garrick, an opportunity
to make a decided hit. He is also to be com-
mended for the artistic feeling which prevented
him returning to bow his acknowledgments for
the ovation accorded him on his exit in the middle
of the first act. Later, a change of costume caused
someone to remark that he only wanted a telescope
to look exactly like Captain Cook, but, fortunately
for him, the gods failed to apprehend the resem-
blance. As Violet Gresham, Mrs. Potter made a
good deal out of a much smaller oppor-
tunity than she is usually afforded, but
her ingenuousness in the first act was too
evidently assumed. Helen Kinnaird, whose role
hitherto has been simply to look lovely and speak
haughtily, as Mrs. Rumbellow showed conclu-
sively that low comedy was her strong point, and
that she was not too enamoured of her good looks
to disguise them temporarily ; and the Selina of
Emma Gwynne was a good companion picture.
“Camille’’has been running during the week to
good houses, and will be displaced on Saturday by
the English version of “ Mdlle. Bressier,” a
story of the Paris Commune. By the way,
we unthinkingly styled scenic-artist Edmonds
“Vaughan ” last week, but this is excusable, as
certain people who know him more intimately than
we do sometimes call him “ Gordon.”

Business improves at Sydney Criterion,
though the “ Bristol ” boom there can never attain
Melbourne dimensions. John E. ’s little sketch ol
the tramp, albeit it is merely one of makeup,
gesture and attitude, is admirable, and his song
“ Miss Mulligan’s home-made pie” is already a
household word. The “Sappho trio” of the
Misses Whiteford, Salinger and Arline is a big
draw for dudes and bald heads, and the Crawford
Bovis high kicking and tumbling makes our bones
ache, Marion and Bell spoil an otherwise good act
by their introduction of the vulgar, humourless,
balloon-bellied business.

At Sydney School of Arts. Maccabe has
changed his programme, and is now playing his
original mellow-drama, “ Virtue Victorious,” in
which lie fights duels with and makes love to him-
self, and, in fact, sustains all the characters from
the gory villain down to the village maiden with
changes in sight of the audience. He has a slight
attack of influenza, but that only adds to the
realism of his snivel in the “Wandering Minstrel.”
As the monologuist points out, they could take in
more people, nevertheless, as times go, the season
is successful.

At Bondi Aquarium the Faust family are
showing to fairly good business, for whenever the
sun shows out the pretty little nook in the cliffs
attracts a big share of the public’s “ooftish.”
Manager Wyburd informs ns that he has arranged
a specially gorgeous programme for Queen’s Birth-
day.

At Darlinghurst Skating Rink business,
