* -* *
The old, old story of J. L. Tooio is drag-
ing along through a seventh chapter at Mel-
bourne Princess’, and tho writing on the wall of
that elegant establishment—where strong shows
flourish like pepper-trees, and weak ones wither
like oysters exposed to a hot wind —can be inter-
preted without the assistance of any Daniel. The
parlous condition of the dress-circle last Saturday
has shocked the management into reverting to a
five-shilling fee for a portion of the house which is
expected to fill, nay, to overflow, on first nights.
But as the three-shilling stalls were likewise
tolerably deserted, it seems improbable that the
half-crown reduction up above will improve
matters. considerable percentage of Saturday’s
circle audience consisted of the Postal Conference
crowd, who came to support Toole as an inte-
gral portion of the British Empire, and a
silken bond binding us to the dear old
mother-land. Deakin, Dan O’Connor,'M’MiJlan,
and their red-faced satellites would have willingly
paid one pound sterling a-piece for the privilege of
flaunting their belief in Hinglish institutions.
They were “distinguished” types of the charac-
ters scattered around, most of whom appeared to
be suffering from a stern sense of Imperial duty.
Half-a-dollar more makes no difference to that
class of customer; nor will two-and-sixpence less
affect ordinary show-goers, who have reckoned up
Toole’s company, once and for all, as the feeblest
lot ever placed upon the boards of a theatre where
excellence in play, or players, has been the pretty
general rule hitherto.
» # *
“The Serious Family” is a loosely-con-
structed chestnut, which many people have
sampled already, and the rest are not likely to
trouble about, Toole's interpretation of Amina-
dab Sleek is amusing, clever, and well-sustained,
but it doesn't—being a comparatively minor part
offer sufficient compensation for the damage
done by those around him. Billington and Miss
Johnstone, the only names worth mentioning, are
not much good in this instance, and the others are
a little worse than the interpolated Miss Constance
Deorwyn, who makes a melancholy attempt to
pose as a giddy child of 16. Miss Deorwyn, how-
ever, has sufficient sense of the fitness of things
to sport a ball-dress which passes muster behind
the footlights, whereas Mr. Toole's leading lady
stalked on in something that looked like a white
cotton sack taken from last week’s washing. This
dingy caricature of a cheap night-gown was
openly asserted to be a rare and costly ornament
by the stage mother of the wearer, who has pro-
bably found time to get it mangled ere now.
Mark Lemon’s farce, “ Domestic Economy,” pro-
vides a lively finish to a very thin entertainment.
Here Toole disguises himself as a tater-digging
farmer in knee-corduroys, but the indelible stamp
of the Cockney is on his whole performance, and
his tater-hoemg is the merest fiction. At time of
writing they talk about reviving “ The Don ” for
Toole's last week, when his old adherents are
bound to rally up and make a respectable attempt
to fill the house. But surely the time has come
when tho “ star ” should consider the moral claims
of the Williamson-Garner firm to utilise some of
their idle, competent hands in place of the —well,
let us say, mediocrities who have wrought such
cruel injury to the present season. A fair all-round
representation of two comedies like “Chawles”
and “ The Don,” with “ Ici ” and “ Off the Line ”
thrown in, might keep the pot boiling vigorously
in Sydney for the appointed six weeks.
* * i!:-
“After Dark” is having a good time at
the Royal, as times go, and may possibly last over
another week. There is a deal of mad waggery
about the serious incidents, a world of humour in
the rescue of a drowning super, from that good old
tank, and a rich fund of merriment in the railway
train. It is quite worth the money to see Herbert
Flemming and Miss Williamson take a midnight
walk arm-in-arm for the purpose of discussing
family matters with a couple of swell-mobsmen
whom they seem to pick up by the roadside.
“ After Dark ” is lurid with the light that never
was on land, or sea, and may be recommended to
all and sundry as a piece of insulting behaviour in
several scenes.

Charles Halle and his gifted wife start
their concert-tour at Melbourne Town Hall this
evening, our old friends Charles Turner and Miss
Annis Montague being extra items in a classic
programme. Such a combination can scarcely fail
to knock the musical world, and if Norman-
Neruda doesn’t fiddle a large audience into a state
of heavenly rapture it will be because the Governor
and suite are conferring their cold, flabby patron-
age upon the occasion.
* *- *
That dearly-beloved insect, “ The Private
Secretary,” is about to suffer further indignities
from Uncle Catterrnole, at the Alexandra. Frank
Thornton, Harwood, Annie Taylor, Brown, Gates,
and the other good people from Sydney are billed
for a popular-prices season of six weeks, com-
mencing on Queen’s Birthday. They may reckon
upon crowded houses all the time, although the
first-night rush will be delayed by the counter-
attraction of a military torchlight-procession,
which is timed to fool around the city just when
the respectable shows are hauling their curtains
up. This procession, moreover, is expected to
make a lot of people too sick to enjoy themselves
afterwards.

Sylvia Grey tried her hand instead of her
toes at a comedy part in “ Pedigree ” produced at
Toole’s Theatre recently. Fair Sylvia was the
semi-serious heroine, and a candid London critic
intimates that she had better stick to the light
fantastic business.
Melbourne theatre-goers have been at a
loss to discover the motive for a sudden outbreak
of insanity in tho local Standard on the subject
of shows. Until lately the issue of treacle, under
the name of theatrical criticism, from that enter-
prising periodical was enough to nauseate.
Suddenly the treacle supply was cut off, and one
night last week there appeared the cranky para-
graph .aforementioned. It started with the trans-
parent misstatement that “ Melbourne theatres
are drawing fair to middling audiences,” when at
least two of them were, and still are, filling
every night. The madness reached a climax in a
ridiculously unjust summing-up of “Paul Jones,”
which all round work of art was alleged to be
merely a poor pantomime, apart from the perform-
ances of Snazelie, Nellie Stewart, John Gourlay,
and Madame Burton. George Leitch, Hudson,
Wood field, and Aggie Kelton, were, for instance,
pantomimists as compared with a subtle artist
Uko G outlay, who is certainly always clever—and
always a clown. Further down in the paragraph
came a miserably weak explanation of its why and
wherefore. “ The Bijou, according to advertise-
ments in morning journals, is doing well.”

“The Ruling Passion,” an incongruous
mass of indescribable claptrap lasting through
five weary acts, has been received at the Adelaide
Royal with shouts of delight by the gallery boys.
Bland Holt, as usual, makes the most of his
opportunities for a gorgeous and realistic scenic
display, and the balloon ascent is a most imposing-
feature, Maggie Knight fairly runs over all her
previous performances in the leading female
character in this dark drama, wherein she has a
most trying part, which taxes her physical ener-
gies to the utmost. Bland Holt, A. Norman, A.
R, Lawrence, A. Glover, H. R. Roberts, Mrs.
Bland Holt, and Nita Steele put in good work
with other characters. Mrs, Gown-Trotter has
postponed her Adelaide season. Harold Ashton
is in Adelaide arranging for the appearance of
Harmston’s Circus,
At Poverty Point.
In the favcc “ New Lamps for Old,” Jerome Iv. Jerome
(who, by the way, scored a bis success with “ Three Men
in a Boat—to say nothin" of the dog,” a book which has
much in it worthy of Mark Twain in his lighter moments)
has struck upon a most whimsical and original situa-
tion, which sets London in a roar. For a certain purpose
one of the characters conceals himself in an hotel lift,
which, after the manner of all lifts, and a good many
clergymen, goes wrong, but in a new direction. It starts
going up and down at an alarming rate, carrying the un-
fortunate wretch with it. For half an hour or so this
continues, while the play goes smoothly on in the fore-
ground, the miserable occupant of the lift coming in
sight every minute or so in a new attitude of rage and
despair.

A Melbourne theatrical scribe lately wrote how he had
once, many years agone, made jelly of Toole’s hat at an
Edinburgh supper. Next post came a note from Johnnie
saying that he “well remembered the incident referred
to,” and hoped that the scribe would call to “ talk over
old times.” The scribbler is doubtful as to whether this
is a Toolian ruse to stick him up for a new hat, with com-
pound interest added at the rate of 10 per cent.
A Melbourne paper says that Sala has found a new
marble man at Orange. This, time the defunct is in a
sitting posture. The last marble man was lying.
A London paper says that Lusoombe Searelle has sold
out his South African interests for £24,000 and gone to
storm London with his opera, “ Bobadil,” which he means
to run for a whole year, whether it pays or not. Searelle
first stormed Australia in the old wooden shod which did
duty for a theatre in Watt-street, Newcastle, as pianist to
a small variety company, which had for its chief attrac-
tion a performing monkey.
Dampier, “ the-Gentleman of the Profession,” stands
high with his theatrical company, in Melbourne, for the
style in which he has kept a large number of people
together for a series of years—actors and actresses,
musicians, stage-hands. This is not at all the general
style of things nowadays. Kudos to Alfred, who has re-
ceived a genuine complimentary benefit.
A sandwich-board man died in London lately, and left
a record of how he had lived on seven shillings a week.
Unfortunately, like the Frenchman’s horse, he died just
when educated to live on one straw a day. At one time
he had a weekly income of £lO, but fell through drink.
Strange to say, the last boards he carried were for “ Tire
Profligate,” a play.

the story and in consequence of the mysterious disap-
pearance of the composer.

Editor Bulletin. Hear Sir,—Some good-
natured friend of mine informs you that I have severed
my connection with tho Herald. This is not correct.
The Herald severed its connection with me. I write this
in sorrow, not in anger : my work for two years past, as
“ Demos,” has been before all Australia. Allow me to
add that the name “Demos” was invented by Editor
Winter, not by myself, as I refused to adopt any ' mra-de-
plume. This may appease some people who have com-
plained that “ Demos ”is no Democrat. “ Demos” is to
me no more than John, but 1 have taken it as an integral
part of my name. I didn’t rush to the Evening Standard
-or anywhere, but wait till the Herald blandly and
affectionately opens its columns again. My service was
only 18 years, not 20 years. Yours truly, Herbbb
Palmer. Herald office, Melbourne, 15th May, 18.00.
Some people are easily amused. Says a
Northern paper
Governor Norman has a good memory for faces. On
his arrival in the colony last June, he had an escort of the
Charters Towers Mounted infantry. He then noticed the
military bearing of some of the men, and, on his arrival
here the other day, asked Captain Russell for Sergeant
Medley. The captain immediately took the Governor
round to the sergeant, with whom the Governor shook
hands, and complimented him on his appearance. It ap-
pears that when the Governor was in Townsville last year
there was considerable difficulty experienced in keeping
the crowd back. One of the crowd caught the bridle of
the horse Sergeant Medley was riding, and clung on to it.
The sergeant quietly dropped the butt-end of his rifle on
the offender’s head, and sent him to the grass. The
Governor, who witnessed the proceeding, was very much
amused, and he apparently had not forgotten it, though
it is nearly 12 months since it occurred.
The joke was, of course, a purely professional
one as also was that perpetrated by the Mel-
bourne surgeon who sewed up the bulldog forceps
inside a living patient.
A Sydney daily paper wishes to know why
the Melbourne suburban lots on whic h the A.M.P.
Society has lent money have not depreciated in
value, while Sydney lots have done so ? For a
journal that is largely in the confidence of real-
estate agents, this desire for information is naive
and innocent—very ! But we’ll gratify it. There
has been no all-round depression in Victorian
property value; there has simply been a collapse of
boomers’ inflations. The few great speculators,
who lived upon each other—witness the Harold
Sparks and James revelations—when the canniba-
listic limit was reached, came tumbling to the
ground like Humpty-Dumpty from his wall. A
great many house and landowners kept their lots
out of the speculators' pool, because they wanted
them for themselves and their children. That is
why their properties have retained their worth.
And why Sydney suburban properties have gone
down is due to the fact that their owners have had
to put them into the market to keep the wolf from
the door. Which the Victorian is -under no
necessity to do.
Hon. J. L. Dow has done a wise thing in
at last resigning his portfolio pending the decision
of the criminal charge preferred against himself,
and his co-directors of Mirams’ Permanent Build-
ing Society’. An imputation of tho kind is so
grave and serious that no Minister of the Crown
against whom it was levelled should have retained
office longer than was necessary to pen his resig-
nation and get it accepted. This much can be
said without any expression of opinion as to the
truth or falsity of the charge. The commonsense
of the community revolts against a Crown
Minister, a man whose hands hold one of the keys
of State, standing in the dock even for half-an-
hour during a preliminary examination. The
Bulletin doesn’t put a high price on the dignity
of office, but it nevertheless believes that a mem-
ber of a Cabinet should be “above suspicion;”
and a police-court criminal summons is rather a
tangible way of expressing doubt as to a man’s
integrity. The Bulletin hopes that Mr. Dow
will again have his portfolio returned to him, and
his resumption of power will be then all the
sweeter because he temporarily abandoned it in
deference to good taste. Both for his own sake
and the sake of the Victorian Ministry does The
Bulletin wish this. Dow, being a successful press -
man, is brainy. The Victorian Ministry minus
the journalists would flop to the intellectual zero.
Verily, brethren, there is a letting in of
the waters and a shaking of the dry bones. The
great triennial parliament of the Australasian
Wesleyan Methodist Church lias spent a solid
week in discussing the retention of the class
meeting as a test of membership. Shades of John
Wesley, of Thomas Jackson, of Jabez Bunting—-
think of that! To put on its trial that holy insti-
tution, this century-old piece of machinery, and to
apply to it the criticism of the modern spirit
is, in truth, a glai’ing sign of the times.
The old-time Methodist thought as much of
the class-meeting as he did of the New Testament,
and almost as much as he did of “Wesley’s
Hymns.” To him it was the means of grace.
Now it has come about that it is only patronised
by the insincere and the hysterical among the
“connection.” One minister after another got
up and declared that the best people in his church
repudiated the class-meeting and all its ennfes-
sional-like characteristics, and that to retain them
in the “connection” he had to report them as
members when, in fact, they were not so-in short,
that lie had to practically “ cook ” his returns in
order that he shouldn’t lose his best people’s con tri

How Muster-Master Stoneman
Earned his Breakfast.*
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
An unpretentious building of rough-hewn
stone standing in the middle of a small, stockaded
enclosure. A doorway in the wall of the building
facing the entrance-gate to the yard. To the left
of the doorway, a glazed window of the ordinary
size. To its right a paneless aperture so low and
narrow that were the four upright and two trans-
verse bars which grate it doubled in thickness no
interstice would be left for the admission of light
or air to the interior. Behind the bars-a face.
Sixteen ho\jrs hence that face will look its last
upon the world which has stricken it countless
cruel blows. In a corner of the enclosure the
executioner’s hand is even now busy stitching into
a shapeless cap, a square of grey serge, the cal-
lous wretch crooning the while lie plies packing-
needle and thread, the monotonous refrain :
Oh, gaily swung he
From the brave gallows-tree.
To-morrow the same hand will use the cap to hood
the face, as one of the few simple preliminaries to
swinging the carcase to which the face is attached
from the rude platform which is in course of
erection against the stockade fence and a bare 20
yards in front of the stone-building.
» # *
The building is the gaol—locally known as
the “cage”—of , a small township in the
midlands of an Australian colony which has
gradually grown up round the convict *’ muster-
station.” The time is five o’clock on a September
evening, 05 years ago. At nine o’clock on the
following morning, Convict Glancy, No. 17,927,
transportee ex ship Pestoujee Bomajee (second
trip), originally under sentence for seven years
for the theft of a silk handkerchief from a London
“swell,” will suffer the extreme penalty of the
law for having, in an intemperate moment, ob-
jected to the mild discipline with which a genial
and loving motherland had sought to correct his
criminal tendencies. In other words, Convict
Glancy, metaphorically goaded by the wordy
insults'aud literally by the bayonet-tip of one of
his motherland’s reformatory agents—to wit,
Road-gang Overseer James Jones —had scattered
J. J.’s brains over a good six square yards of
metalled roadway. The deed had been raptur-
ously applauded by Glancy’s fellow-gangers, all of
whom had the inclination, but lacked the courage,
to wield the crowbar that had been the means of
erasing their particular tyrant’s name from the pay-
sheets of His Britannic Majesty’s Colonial Penal
Establishment. Nevertheless and notwithstanding
such tribute of appreciation, H.B.M.’s Colonial
representatives, police, judicial and gubernatorial,
thought it rather one to be censured and had,
accordingly, left Convict Glancy for execution.
This decision of the duly-constituted authorities
that person had somewhat irrelevantly (as it will
seem to us at this enlightened day) acknowledged
by a fervent “Thank God !”-—an ejaculation the
more remarkable as never before in his convict
history had he ever linked the name of the Deity
with any expression of gratitude for tin- many
blessings that he enjoyed in that state of
penal servitude to which it had pleased that same
Deity to call him. On the contrary, he had con-
stantly indulged in maledictions on his fate and on
his Maker. He had lesolutely cursed the be-
nignant forces with which the System and the
King’s Regulations had surrounded him, and he had'
failed to reverence as he ought the triangles, the
gang-chains, the hominy, the prodding bayonet,
and the other things which.would have conduced
to his reformation if he had manifested a more
humble and obedient spirit. No wonder, there-
fore, as Chaplain Ford said, that it had come
about that he had qualified for the capital doom.
Upon this doom, in so far as it could be repre-
sented by the gallows, Convict Glancy was now
gazing with an unflinching eye. On this Septem-
ber evening he stood at his cell-window looking on
half-a-dozen brown-clothed figures handling saw,
and square, and hammer, as they fix in the earth
a sturdy upright, and to that a projecting cross-
beam ; as they bind the two with a solid tie-piece
of knotless hardwood ; as they build a narrow
platform of planks around the gallows-tree; as they
fasten a rope to the notched end of the cross-beam;
and as they slope to the edge of the planks, ten
feet from the ground, a rude ladder. All the.
drowsy afternoon he had watched the working
party, though Chaplain Ford hud stood by his
side droning of the grace which had been withhold
from him in life, but might still be his in death.
He had felt interested, had Convict Glancy, in
these preparations for the event in which he was
to act such a prominent part on the morrow. He
had even laughed at the grim humour of one of
the brown-garbed workers who, when the warder’s
eye was off him, had gone through the pantomime
of noosing the rope-end round his own neck—a
little joke which contributed much to the (neces-
sarily noiseless) delight of the rest of the gang.
Altogether, Convict Glancy reflected as dusk
fell, and the working party gathered up their
tools, and the setting sun tipped the bayonets of
the guard with a diamond iridescence, that he
had spent many a duller and more trying after-
noon. If the Chaplain had only held his tongue,
the time would have passed with real pleasantness.
He said as much to the good man as the latter re-
marked to the warder on duty in the cell that he
would look in again after supper.
“You may save yourself the trouble, sir,"
quoth, respectfully enough, Convict Glancy.
“ You have spoilt my last afternoon. Don’t spoil
my last night! ’
Chaplain Ford winced at the words. He was
still comparatively new to the work of spiritually
superintending a hundred or so monsters who
looked upon the orthodox hell as a place where
residence would be pleasantly recreative after the
Settlement and Norfolk Island; and the time lay
still in the future when, being completely cm-
Imitod, he would come to regard it as a very
curious circumstance indeed that Christ had
emitted eulogistic reference to the System from
the Sermon on the Mount. Consequently he
winced and sighed, not so much-Atq do him jus-
tice—at the utter depravity of Convict Clancy as
at his own inability to reach the reprobate’s heart.
But he took the hint; he mournfully said he
would not return that evening, but would be-with
the prisoner by half-past 5 o’clock in the morning.
# # *
When Chaplain Ford entered the enclosure
immediately before the hour he had named, ho at
once understood, from the excitement manifested
by a group assembled in front of the “cage,”
that something was amiss. Voices were uttering
impetuously, almost shriekiugly, fearful words,
and hands swung lanterns—the grey dawn had
not yet driven the darkness from the stockade—and
brandished muskets furiously. A very brief space
of time served to inform the revei’end functionary
what had gone wrong.
Convict Clancy had made his escape, having
previously murdered, with the victim’s own
bayonet, the warder who had been told-.off to
watch him during the night. This latter circum-
stance was, of course, unfortunate, but alone it
would not have created the excitement, for the
murder of prison-officials was a common enough
occurrence. It was the other thing that galled
the gesticulating and blaspheming group. That a
prisoner, fettered with ten-pound chains, should
have broken out of gaol on the very eve of his
execution —why, it was calculated to shake the
confidence of the Penal Commandant himself in
the infallibility and perfect righteousness of the
System. And if popular and authoritative belief
in the System was shattered, where would they be ?
The murdered man had gone ou duty at 10
o'clock, and very shortly after that he must have
met with his fate. How Clancy had obtained
possession of the bayonet could only be conjec-
tured. As was the custom during the day or two
preceding a convict’s execution, he had been left
unmanacled, and ironed with leg-chains only.
Thus his hands were free to perpetrate the deed
once lie grasped the weapon. Clancy, on his
escape, had taken the instrument with him, but
there was no doubt that he had inflicted death
with it, the wound in the dead man’s breast being
obviously caused by the regulation bayonet.
Possibly the sentinel had nodded, and —a sudden
VERE HAD IT GONE?

wrench of the prisoner’s wrist and a sudden stab
had extended his momentary slumber into an
eternal sleep. The bayonet had also been used by
Clancy to prise up a flooring-flag, and then to
scoop out mu aperture under the wall, the base
stones of which, following the slipshod architec-
ture of the time, rested ou the surface and
were not sunk into the ground. The work
of excavation must have taken the convict several
hours, and must have been conducted as noise-
lessly as the manner of committing the crime it-
self. A solitary warder occupied the outer guard-
room. but he assorted that he had heard no sound
except ill" exchange of whistle-signals between
the dormitory guard at the convict-barracks (a
qiiarter-of-milc away at the rear of the gaol-
stockade) and the military patrol. The. night
routine of the “cage” did not insist upon the
whistle-signal between the men on duty, but they
passed a simple “All's well” every hour. And
this the guard room-warder maintained lie had
done with the officer inside the condemned cell,
the response being given in a low tone, from con-
sideration, so the former thought, for the sleeping
convict so soon to die. Of course, if this man was
to be believed, Clancy must have uttered the
words. K was not the first time the signal which
should have been given by a prison officer had been
made by Iris convict murderer.
The murder was discovered on the arrival of the
relief watch at live o’clock. The last ‘ All well
was exchanged at four. Consequently the escapee
had evidently less than an hour’s start. The
scaling of the stockade would not bo difficult oven
for a man in irons, and once in the bush an experi-
enced hand would soon find a method of fracturing
the links.
It must be admitted that this contumacious
proceeding of Convict Clancy was most vexa-
tious. Under-Sheriff Hopewell, now soundly
reposing at the township inn, would bo forth-
coming at 9 o’clock with his Excellency’s warrant
nr Ins hand to demand from Muster-Master Stone-
man the body of one James Glaney, and Muster-
Master Stoneman would have to apologise f° r hi.
inability to produce the said body. Thedithcn y
was quite unprecedented, and Stoneman, as ne
stood in the midst of his minions, groaned audibly
at the prospect of having to do the thing most
abhorrent to the official mind establish a piece
“Such a thing was never heard of!’ he cried.
“A man to bolt just when he was to be turned
off ! And the d d hypocrite tried to make Ins
Honor and all of ns think he was only too happy
to bo scragged. It's too d——d bad !
It certainly did seem peculiar that Clancy, who
had apparently much rejoiced at the contempla-
tion of his early decease, should give leg-bail just
when he was to realise his wishes. He bad told
the judge that “ he was glad they were going
to kill him right off instead of by inches, and yet
he had voluntarily thrown off the noose when it
was, virtually round his neck. Was it the mere
“cussedness ” of the convict nature that prmnptea
the escape? Or, was it the innate love ot me that
becomes stronger as the benefits of living become
fewer and fewer ? Had the craving for existence
and for freedom surged over his despair and reck-
lessness at the eleventh hour? , . ~ , ,
Such were the inquiries which Chaplain I qrd put
to himself as, horrified, he took in the particulars
of No. 17,927’s crowning enormities from the
hubbub of the group. ■ , , ,
“Damn it!” said the Muster-Master at last,
“we are losing time. The devil can’t have gone
far with those ten-pounders on him. I swear i
won’t touch food this day till I get him. VVe 11
have to put the regulars on the track as well as
our own men. Warder Briggs, report to Captain
White at the barracks, and—”
Muster - Master Stoneman stopped short.
Through the foggy air there came the familiar
sound as of a convict dragging his irons. vv hat
could it be? No prisoners had been as yet loosed*
from the dormitory. Whence could the noise

Clink-clank— s-sh dr-g-g clink clank
dr-g-g. The sound drew nearer, and Convict
Clancy turned in at the enclosure gateway un-
escorted. He had severed the leg-cham at the
link which connected with the left anklet, but
had not taken the trouble to remove the other
part of the chain. Thus, while he could take his
natural pace with his left foot, he dragged the
fetters behind his right leg.
A moment of hushed surprise, and then three
or four men rushed towards him. The first that
touched him he felled with a blow.
“ Not yet,” said he, grimly. “I give myself
up, Mr. Stoneman —you don’t take me!” And
then Convict Clancy laughed, and held out his
hands fur the handcuffs. He laughed more
heartily as the subordinate hirelings of the System
threw themselves upon him like hounds on their
prey.
“No need to turn out the sodgers now, Muster-
Master—-not till nixie o'clock.” Once more his
hideous laugh rang through the yards. “ You
had an easier job than you expected, hadn’t you,
Stoneman, old cove?”
Muster-Master Stoneman had been surprised
into silence and into an unusual abstinence from
blasphemy by the re-appearance quite unprece-
dented under the circumstances -of the doomed
wretch. But the desperado’s jeering tones
whipped him into speech.
“Danin you!” ho yelled. “I’ll teach you to
laugh on the other side of your mouth presently.
You’d better have kept away.” He literally
foamed in his mad anger.
“ Do you think I couldn’t have stopped away if
I'd wanted to, having'got clear? A lofty scorn
rang out in the words. “ Hut do you think I was
going to run away when I was so near Freedom as
that?” And the wretch jerked Ids manacled
hands in the direction of the gallows. “You
d d fool !”
No one spoke for a full half-minute. .Chen :

As Muster-Master Stoneman turned on his heel
to prepare the warrant for the flogging, he looked
at his watch. It was half-past six,
* # #
At seven o’clock the first lash from the
oat-o’-nino-tails fell upon Convict Clancy’s back.
At 8.30 his groaning and bleeding body, which
had received the full hundred of flaying stripes,
lay on the-pallet of the cell where he had murdered
the night-guard but a few hours before.
At 8.45 Under-Sheriff Hopewell, who bad been
apprised while at breakfast of the murder and
escape, appeared on the scene escorted by Ins
javelin-men. This gentleman, too, had been
greatly perplexed by Convict Clancy’s proceed-
ings. “Really it was most inconsiderate of the
man,” lie said to the Muster-Master, “1 do not
know whether I ought to proceed to execution,
pending his trial for this second murder.”
“Oh,” said the latter functionary—flicking with
his handkerchief from his coat-sleeve as he spoke
xi drop of Convict Clancy’s blood that had fallen
there from a reflex swirl of the lash; “your
duty is clear. You must hang him at nine o'clock,
and try him afterwards for the last crime. ”
And as Convict Clancy, ex Pestonjee Bomajee
(second), No. 17,927, was punctually hanged at
9.5, it is to be presumed that the Under-Sheriff
had accepted this solution of the difficulty.
At 10.15 a mass of carrion having been huddled
into a shell, and certain coronial formalities hav-
ing been duly attended to, Muster-Master Stone-
man bethought himself that he had not break-
fasted.
“ I’ll see you later, Mr. Hopewell,” he said, as
the latter was endorsing the Governor’s warrant
with the Coroner’s verdict ; “ I’m going to break-
fast. I think I’ve earned it this morning.”

A True Bill.
Dr. Morgan T. Wills, a recognised American
authority on cerebral disease and insanity, writes :
“ It is well for us to know that the emotions cause
mole unhappiness and crime than any other
function of the brain. Human beings are governed
by their emotions, and it is well that they should
be, though it is emotions, such as anx'ety, fear,
sorrow and love that wear away the brain.
I consider that eight hours are sufficient
for a man to use his brain, because if he exceeds
that time he becomes nervous and fretful, and
an exhausted brain is an irritable brain. You
may not feel the evil effects of the stress of brain
work at the time, but you will sooner or later,
when it will be too late. The men that work at
night with their brains are the ones that expose
themselves to danger and death, which will surely
come unless the great strain on the mind is
lightened.
Any man that neglects the first warning of a
brain or nervous system that is becoming ex-
hausted, overtaxed or about to break down, is not
only a fool but a criminal. These signs are not
many, but they tell the story of coming dangers
only too plainly. Headache, sleeplessness, irrita-
bility of temper, neuralgic pains about the head
and heart, unrefreshful sleep, nervous dyspepsia,
dull eyes, heaviness of the head, and stupid feel-
ing after meals, worry about tribes, unreasonable
anger, tingling and numbness in the limbs, cold
feet and hands, Bushed face and burning ears,
palpitation of the heart, and irregular, weak and
unsteady pulse. When you note these symptoms
beware; the brain and nerves are about to break
down, and it may be insanity, perhaps death.”
For all such troubles we recommend Clements
Tonic. This is a scientific specific, brain and
nerve food, and positively repairs the ravages of
overwork, time, disease, &c.
If Clements Tonic was not genuine, would the
Kiama Reporter call it “The remedy of the
day,” or the Tamworth News say it was “ A
specific for all diseases of debility,” or the Goul-
burn Post say it was “a Radical Cure,” ortho
Newtown Chronicle say it was “a remedy of
approved efficacy,” or the Nepean Times call it
“a really first-class Tonic,” or the Macleav
Argus say “It is a reliable article,” or The
Bulletin say “It is a remedy of the highest
value,” or the Grafton Grip say “Praise is
superfluous,” or the Bingera Telegraph desig-
nate it “A valuable medicine,” or the Presby-
terian say, “It can be confidently prescribed?'
Yet all these papers have printed these words in
connection with Clements Tonic. The Press is
far to conservative to endorse an unproved article,
but we can show hundreds of similarly eulogistic
comments from the press similar to the above.
We don't need to guarantee when wo can get
disinterested parties to speak of Clements Tonic
as the journalists do.
Send for our Medical Guide, free on receipt of
your address, F, M. Clements, Newtown, Sydney.

This is an authoritative pronouncement by
a U.S. cardinal :
A Oatliolic must not only believe what the Church now
proposes for his belief, but be ready to believe whatever
she may hereafter propose. And he must, therefore, be
ready to give up any or all of hie probable opinions as
soon'as they are condemned and proscribed by competent
authority.
Just link this together with the cabled statement
of last week that the Pope contemplates making
the acceptance of his temporal power a matter of
dogma, and a portentous result is arrived at.

Personal Items.
Home Ruler Dillon is an L.R.C.P.
The Dailey memorial is still in futurity.
Pastor Jelferis says : “ Australia cleaves to
me.” How much of it?
The Feed’em of the City of London ha s
been conferred upon Stanley.
Michael Davitt will conduct a new London
paper called the Larouk World.
The difference in weight between Lady
I lopctoun and Lady Oarington is 421bs.
Mr. Whittaker, late chief of the reporting
.staff, is the new sub-editor of the Melbourne
Idvknina Standard.
Willoughby Andrew has now, it is said,
entirely recovered his reason. But for all that lie
will have to stay in gaol for life.
Should Speight resign the Victorian Rail-
way Commissioner ship, at least three members of
the Assembly will struggle for the billet.
Naturalists assert that the tongue of the
giraffe is a foot and a-half long. A. G. Taylor is
lovingly known in Mudgee as the “ Giraffe.”
Explorer Stanley is going to be married.
Before he gives his hand to any white lady let us
hope he will wash off his hands the black blood.
Edward Skinner the well-known Sydney
bookseller, is one of the oldest pioneers of Port
Phillip, having gone there with immigrants in 1840.
The late Archbishop Vaughan gave George
Robertson a standing order for all volumes of ser-
mons, no matter what school of thought they came
from.
Mr. 0. J. Roberts, C.M.G., one of the
leading lights of the N.S.W. liquor-trade, says
that drunkenness should be a solid reason for
divorce.
A Rockhampton paper says that “ the
charm of Governor Norman’s manner seems to
tone down even the habitual bearishness of his
Ministers.”
Captain Webber, who commanded the ill-
fated Ly-ee-moon when she was wrecked at Green
(Jape some years ago, now superintends a N.S.W.
country hospital.
Dr. E. M. James, who goes to Europe,
thus severs a 37 years’ connection with Melbourne
Hospital. The Doctor was amongst the lucky
ones at Broken Hill.
Rev. D. M. Ross, airing his Australian
experiences in the Dundee Advertiser, comments
on the absence of such excellent Christian com-
forts as twopenny drinks.
Akhurst, the pantomime writer, while on
the Melbourne Press, used to delight in writing
half-a-dozen articles at once, on the most diverse
subjects. It kept him from gushing.
A music-hall cantatrice, who arrived in
Melbourne last year, seems to have changed her
name because it was too much like that of the
most famous female pickpocket in the city.
A Melbourne civil servant, who recently
retired on the grounds of “ lengthened service and
senile decay,” has just got married to a sweet
youngthiugand is honeymooning on a fat pension.
Rudyard Kipling is a wonderful new
young 'writer, on Indian subjects, particularly
military life and character. A volume of his
sketches has made a bigger hit even than did
Marion Crawford’s ‘“Dr. Isaacs.”
The Mayor of Hawthorn (Vic.) has been
made godfather to a horse. It isn’t clear for which
of the parties -the horse or the mayor—the com-
pliment was intended. The horse draws the local
tire-engine to scenes of conflagration and beer.
Professor Drummond, the explorer and
metaphysician, now on a visit to Melbourne, and
the late'Rev. J. T. Ewing, of Toorak, wen; school-
mates. The Professor witnessed Ewing’s will in
Glasgow many years ago. He came to Australia
just in time to attest his codicil.
A certain well-known N.Z. editor and
Exhibition man has a singularly cherubic face and
a pair of colossal ears. “ Look here, Blank,” said
brother-editor Gillon to him one day, “if you cut-
off your ears and stuck them on your shoulders
you’d make a d d fine angel. ”
A small weekly paper, published at a
country town within twenty miles of Sydney, is
edited by an inmate of a, local asylum for the
aged and helpless. He is a University man
(wrangler 8.A.) and a member of the English
Bar, and he is certainly a clever writer.
Queen Victoria has unveiled yet another
statue to Albert the Good. In 20 years the
British public will look at his numerous brazen
effigies, and not knowing anything much about
the man, will wonder what he was good for. Then
they will boil him down and make pennies of him.
Knatchbull, Mrs, Jamieson’s murderer,
was related by marriage to Sir Joseph Banks, who
accompanied Captain Cook to Australia. The
N.S.W. Agent-General recently purchased old
colonial papers from the Knatchbull family which
are now in the possession of the Colonial Secretary.
George Swanson, after whom Swanston-
street, the busy thoroughfare of Melbourne, was
named, is still hale, hearty and happy at the age
of 80 years. He arrived in Victoria 50 years ago,
and was present at the burial of Batman. The
“t” is wrongfully used in the spoiling of the
street,
Sara Bernhardt called at the head London
police-office about some stolen jewels, and insisted
on seeing the Chief of Police. An officer assured
her that she could not possibly see that functionary
without an appointment. “Why not? Why, f
can see the Prince of Wales without an appoint-
ment !” replied Sara.
Alfred Woolley, the lately-deceased Mel-
bourne merchant, arrived there in 1830, made a
fortune, went to England to see the Exhibition
of 1851, and was enticed back by the gold fever
He did not leave behind him over-much gold,
after 50 years’ work, but he leaves a golden name,
for no poor man or woman ever asked his assist-
anee in vain.
Lloyd, proprietor of the London Daily
Chronicle and of Lloyd’s Newspaper, was a
very remarkable man. He was over 70 when ho
created this new Chronicle, by infusing £150,000
into it, combined with a million's worth of brains.
He grew the esparto grass for his paper at his big
farm in Algeria, which supplied two Melbourne
papers as well.
Winter Irving, who has inexplicably be-
come transformed from Irving Winter, is a Vic-
torian peer, and lias given Sandhurst a piece of
statuary, by Ohas. Summers, representing a
mother and daughter fleeing from Pompeii. Sum-
mers is a native, of Melbourne, and works at
Rome, but he has not the genius of his departed
father, nor has Winter the genius of the late
“Jock.”
After a long and troublous life, Thadeus
O’Kane is dead. He was educated originally for
the R.C. priesthood, but turned out from the path
leading to that Church to strike out a rough bridle-
track of his own. He was a vigorous writer, and
one of the most independent journalists who ever
trod Australian soil. In his nature there was all
the wit of a clever priest and all the gall of a boss
Orangeman.
A well-known man now Hying in Sydney
had once the misfortune to look upon the rum
when it was red. One night during his spree he
was sitting up in bed with a loaded pistol, which
he kept ever by his side. The “ gun ” was loaded
and he was irresistibly tempted to fire at a mos-
quito which happened to alight on his foot, and
which he at the time and under the circumstances
took to be a kangaroo. Ho now goes about on one
leg only.
For the rising generation, which still
goes to the circus, the story of Marcus Clarke
treating old Birnie to a dinner at the Melbourne
Cafe de Paris may be exhumed. Marcus’

POLITICIAN (to applicant for Government billet): “MY GOOD MAN, I WOULD DO
AS MUCH FOR YOU AS IF YOU WERE MY OWN BROTHER!”
APPLICANT (joyfully): “OH, SIR! I NEVER EXPECTED SUCH KINDNESS"

“ regular ” meal was an eighteen-penny square one
there, good enough for the German Emperor. He
met Birnie outside, and asked him in intending to
provide an eighteenpenny one for him, too. As
soon as the Grand Old Classic was fairly planted,
he began to find out there were turtle soup, teal,
Chateau Margaux, early strawberries, and what
not, and Marcus had to part a guinea. Said
Birnie to a friend : “I have just dined, and to
think I should be indebted for it to that boy !”
We protest vigorously against the story, but there
it is.
Mr. Justice Mein was never a barrister,
but only a solicitor. By Queensland law all
divisions of the legal profession are amalgamated,
but by Queensland legal etiquette—the close guild
law of the Queensland Law Society—the barristers
hold themselves several cuts above (in quality, but
not often in earnings) the solicitors. Sir Samuel
Griffith, Q.C., has always been the chief con-
servator of the alleged “ rights ” of barristers, and
the leader in upholding the caste differences of
the profession : nevertheless, it was Sam Griffith,
the Premier, who turned his back on Sam Griffith,
I he lawyer, and broke through the guild etiquette
by appointing Mein, then Minister of J ustice, a
Judge. Sam's reply to his horror-stricken legal
confreres was —“lt is the law !” and the Devil’s
Brigade had to swallow it. Mein was a member
of the firm of Hart, Mein and Flower, and was
always regarded as a fair-spoken man. Lately he
has been much ravaged by military ardour and
rheumatics, which may account for a whole lot of
things.

“ Bullets and the Bible must civilise
Africa,” says Chancellor Caprivi. Bullets come
first, but where does the Rum come in ?
Never, never, will there be a real alliance
between Republican France and Despotic Russia.
The tyrants will bo left to join up together.
The Age alludes to the Australian natives’
Association as “The A.N. Ass.” From this we
conclude that David Syme now scorns Purvesian
grovel and means to make the Age a Republican
paper.
Purves, Q.C., said in court on the first
day of the Premier Permanent case that he would
prove “ beyond the possibility of a doubt ’ that
Minister Dow’s “ connection with the swindle was
not honest.”
Lord Hartington states that he has no
desire to rejoin the Gladstonian party while it
encourages the views of English Socialists. And
nobody doubts that the future Duke of Devonshire
is speaking the solemn truth.
Parson Haunay, back from Australia, has
punched it into the London Congregational Union
that Australia insists on independence. No go,
he says, the half-measures. Just the same as it
was iu the United Sates a hundred years ago.
The venerable Dr. Knaggs, after nearly 40
years’ practice in Melbourne, did not die rich.
His estate was valued at £(1540, but then the Doc-
tor’s hand was constantly in his pocket, for he
gave money and food as well as advice and medi-
cine to hundreds of people.
The Grand Old Man’s mistaken ideas re-
garding Chinese virtues are accounted for by the
fact that he has never encountered that great
nation, save iu the form of Chinese ambassadors.
We should like to take Gladstone for a turn round
Lower George-street, Sydney, or Lilly Buck-
street, Melbourne, and then ask him whether
Chow virtue was too strony for Christian com-
petition.
Bamboiu'uc comes down in Punch on the
German Ka’ser. The monarch enters a black-
smith's shop with a basket of tools on his back
and the Crown yn his head. Says he to a Cyclops
hammering at an anvil- “I’m one of you.”
“Are you? ” says Cyclops, “ but you’ll have to
drop'your Crown first.” That is v/hat Bismarck
clearly perceives. It is fight or run. No com-
promise.
Of the fifteen lawyers in the N.S.W.
Legislative Council 13 voted in the division on
the Divorce Bill, seven for and five against. Of
the other throe, Septimus Stephen is in England,
Long was absent, and Salomons stepped into
the President’s gallery during the division.
Two ex-judges did not “stand by a brother
judge,” and all the Roman Catholics in the House
voted loudly with the noes.
It is rumoured that knighthoods are to be
conferred on Colton and Playford, also on the
democratic Premier, Cockburn, all of S.A.; also
on Morehead of Queensland; Pearson of Vic-
toria; and M‘Millan of N.S.W. The acceptance
of Imperial titles was not a matter of much
political significance in the bygone days when
ouug Australia was in his swadling clothes.
Their acceptance to-day means that the politician
bebaubled is an enemy to Republicanism, and an
adorer of the Imperial purple.
Sir Charles Dilke has annoyed the Imperial
swaslibucklers by suggesting that Australia is
practically beyond the reach of invasion by any
foreign power. True, O ! Charles. Hingland
runs the only navy which might, possibly, swoop
down upon this country in overwhelming force.
And the holders of Australian bonds will take
good care that Hingland doesn’t ever dream of
blowing away their interest-coupons. TFcVt all
right.
Democrats are always very jealous of tam-
pering with the Post-office. Some diamonds, sent
through the mail from abroad, were lately de-
tained by the Victorian postal authorities. This
kind of tiling is apt to lead to further developments,
as in the famous Stevenson Custom House cases,
when not Only were all George Stevenson’s London
letters detained, but also the private letters of his
wife and daughter. Those letters would have
been opened but for the public outcry.
Sydney Echo claims that flogging was the
means of abolishing garotting in London. If it
will look up Mr., P. A. Taylor’s Commons speech
on the subject, and the statistics therein, it will
have to admit at once that it has been building an
argument on a long-exploded myth. Flogging
did not put down garotting in London any more
than the gallows lias put down murder in
England. . Another favourite yarn of the Echo’s
is that capital punishment has been re-enacted in
Switzerland,
They officially burned the Christmas num-
ber of Foote’sblasphemous ” Freethinker in
Melbourne ; now the cry is taken up in N.S. W.,
and anyone publishing it is threatened with
“ blasphemy ” prosecution by the Colonial Secre-
tary. How moral Australia is, to be sure ! The
paper is circulated in England without let or
hindrance—but in this “ freer ” country its sale
means penal servitude. What would the clergy
not prohibit were the power theirs ? Despite their
efforts, Australia will yet be so free that people
will be at liberty to publicly worship Judas Iscariot
if they like.
The curious thing about the Anti-Divorce
agitation in Victoria is that the Act should have
been allowed to pass almost without pretest, and
now the mischief is all done, the clergy arc making
themselves ridiculous, urging rebellion against the
law of the land, as sanctioned by Her Gracious
and Sacred Majesty Queen Victoria, Vice-gerent
of God and Defender of the , Faith. Canon
Chalmers, celebrating a fashionable marriage (a
ten-guinea job of joy) in a crowded church, took
occasion to make an-Onslaught on Her Sacred
Majesty’s Law of the Land, worthy of Father
Slathery inveighing at Geelong against the Edu-
cation Act.

that the Victorian Upper House “ will stand as
a dam in flood-time,” and thinks that “ if the
H ousts of Lords is not strong enough in Great
Britain for this purpose then assuredly there
•should be some other protection." The Argus
doesn’t suggest what the “ extra” should be. A
“ House of Royal Princes,” wo suppose? Then a
“Grand Bench of Hereditary Idiots;” and,
filially, a “ ,Sacred Temple of Mummies with at-
tached Automatic Phuno-telc-mano-auvo-smclio-
graphs.” As for the Victorian Legislative Council
“ standing as a dam,” the united damns of an op-
posed people would soon obliterate its dam pre-
sence.
X.S. \V. Attorney-(loneml Simpsons
Criminal Law Amendment Bill may be described
as a give-and-take measure. It proposes to give
the judges more discretionary power as to the
length of sentences. Should this provision bo
passed, as it undoubtedly will, the penalties im-
posed on prisoners convicted under the present
order of things should in justice be reviewed. It
also proposes to ensure the flogging of everybody
sentenced to the lash. Just now people ordered
to be tied up on certain dates to the triangles
often escape by reason of their being too sick to be
flogged. The Simpsonian proposition is presum-
ably to patch them up and flog them afterwards —
the limit being six months from date of conviction.
Which proposal will be received with hilarious
applause by the old Botany Bay element of the
community.
Dual' Bulletin, —The recent disaster at
Bourke—viz., the bursting of the dam —points
most uumistakeably to the utter futility of
attempting to meet the “ subtle advances of an
insidious foe ” with an undisciplined horde and no
leader. Of what use was it—the pouring hun-
dreds of navvies into Bourke without an engineer
to direct their work ? Fancy setting gangs of
navvies to work under Alderman So-and-So !
There was a thundering lot of rushing about and
any amount of “graft,” but of engineering skill
and the proper utilisation of that labour—well,
the less said the better.—Cur Bono.
The French campaign in Dahomey has
roused up a pathetic story of how a miserable
African rushed to destruction some fifty years
ago. In these days an enlightened scalp-hunter
sat on the Dahomau throne, and he conceived the
idea of establishing a bomb-proof army, and with
this end in view he ordered a quantity of im-
pervious breastplates for the use of his troops.
When they arrived he put a sample breastplate on
the prime minister, and then fired a cannon at
him; and when the smoke gradually cleared
away the remains of a foot were discovered on the
top of a tall palm tree, also about a pint of but-
tons, some hair, and the ruins of an eye. It said
a good deal for the late monarch’s intelligence
that lie used a politician to experiment on, instead
of wasting some citizen who had a greater chance
of being missed : and the example he set is worthy
of imitation. The Australian can muster seven
prime ministers in all, and if a cannon were fired at
six of them some valuable discoveries in gunnery
might ensue. It wouldn't matter much which six
were selected either, provided the camion didn’t
get damaged

Society
Baron Carington will not eat his Christmas
dinner in Sydney. Of the 20 servants lie brought
out few return with him. Like Jack of the fleet,
they are enamoured of the freer life prevailing in
the Sunny South.
# # *
Of late the Hopetouns have been going in
heavily fur the Melbourne theatres and other
inexpensive kinds of vice-regal duties. They
have taken a stroll round the hospitals and a
ramble through the Zoological Gardens at feeding
tune. But to very little purpose have the small
Scotch lordship and his bonnie lassie endeavoured
to give the gubernatorial prestige a shaking up.
As sassiety loadstones they are a dreary failure,
and as objects of popular enthusiasm they rank
with General Gordon’s neglected statue.
The Bulletin learns that the Admiral
of the Station—everybody will here please turn
round three times —is terribly indignant
over the extremely disloyal attitude, don’t-you-
know, of the people of N.S. Wales in connection
with the Egeria episode. The Admiral - turn
round three times—is doubtful whether it is better
to mark the sense of his displeasure by recom-
mending the removal of the naval head-quarters
to a more loyal latitude, or by merely issuing a
ukase that in future naval court-martials be con-
ducted at Kerguelen Island or at the Chathams.
“It will prove so very bad for Sydney in a
commercial sense, you know,” said a naval officer
to a Bulletin man the other day.
* * *
Sydney men won’t be sorry to see the last
of the fleet. Its gallant officers have nothing to
do but mash our best girls, to say nothing of the
married women.
The mayors, councillors, and similar official
fry of Victoria in general, and Melbourne in par-
ticular, are shaking their gory locks and saying
“Did you ever?” in a loud, representative tone of
voice. With a stroke of his secretary’s pen ’is
Hexcellency Hopetoun has denuded these good
people of their ancient right and privilege of roll-
ing up to a levee in one fat lump and being for-
mally introduced to the Lord’s Anointed by the
boss of their body corporate. They formerly stood
on pedestals apart, as it were, from the common,
or garden, variety of levee-stormer. After clutch-
ing the sacred fist of deputy-majesty in this private
and confidential kind of way, they usually snorted
three times by way of insinuating that they had
got there by an extra superior route. But on the
approaching anniversary of Victoria Regina’s
nativity “ the English rule will be observed and
each gentleman attending will be presented in the
Same manner as are the general public, by the
officer of His Excellency’s household, who is de-
puted for that purpose.” As this new arrange-
ment is likely to knock the gilt off a lot o : effusive
“loyalty,” and seems eminently calculated to
shake the faith of many well-to-do, stout gentle-
men in Hold Hinglish Hinstitutions, The Bulle-
tin regards it as a distressing move in the right
direction.
# # #
On the whole Earl Onslow has earned a
very decent reputation since he was transported to
New Zealand. But then New Zealanders as a
body are intensely Conservative, or else they have
been favoured with very decent and well-behaved
Governors. They knew Bowen—saw through
him and patted him on the back ; they rather ad-
mired Robinson, the horsey men worshipped him ;
they liked the old “Markiss.” He was a very
popular old fellow in his time. They absolutely
doated on Jervois, and if Sir George Grey had
carried his Bill (it will be carried yet) for elective
Governors, Jervois would have been elected if he
had stood —and he would have done that. Onslow
is making a name for himself, and bids fair
to be very popular indeed. Now, why is this
thus? Simply because eacli of these Governors has
been and is a man amongst men. There is little
Kotooing in New Zealand to Governor or anybody
else. They are a bluff, outspoken folk, these same
New Zealanders, and a Governor is treated just
as any other respectable, well-behaved man would
lie—neither more nor less. And the Governors
quickly, and naturally, and with personal pleasure,
drop into the groove that the colonists assign them.
But one unpopular Governor would break the
Imperial knot ere the Imperial authorities could
gasp one single sob.

From the Melbourne Heeald :
Tl/'IDOWEHS observe. Several widows of good
moral characters, with comfortable home, wait-
ing introduction. View Matrimony.
This sounds very well—bnt wouldn't it be more
satisfactory if these widows could produce certifi-
cates of character from their former husbands?
In our opinion they should be compelled to do so
before re-marrying. There is a great opportunity
here for an ambitious member of Parliament who
wishes to do a good thing in his generation, to
bring in a Bill for the Protection of the Male Sex
from Widows. Duncan Gillies himself might do
worse than take it in hand,
* * #
1 n the course of a discussion on a proposed
Bachelor Tax, held lately by the Essendon (Vic.)
branch of the Australian Natives’ Association,
one of the speakers pointed out that 105 per cent,
of the ladies of the colonies got married. And yet
they are not satisfied ! Widows are at the bottom
of this trouble also.
* # #
Somebody, writing to a Melbourne paper,
states that a strike of bank-clerks is one of the
possibilities of the near future. A strike of white
mice ! If such a miracle did occur the directors of
banks in Melbourne would only have to send to
the Model Lodging-houses and the Eight o’clock
Rush to get scores of men to fill the vacant places.
These men would be mostly middle-aged and, very
probably, smelling of liquor, but they would know
their business—being all ex-bank-clerks who had
“ floated out ” (on a flood of beer) from their billets
years ago. It would require another miracle to
sober them up, of course, but if we are going in for
miracles why stop at one ?

wooden-legged boy who holds his own with the

of one of The Bulletin canvassers, and a Bulle-
tin canvasser never tells a lie.

H police-court, proceedings in Melbourne
Ihe other day, the Bench let a barmaid witness
“leave the precincts ” and return to her bar, be-
cause “ the bar has to bo closed in the meantime,
and Lord knows how many thirsty people are
being put about.” That was good. " An hour
later, a boarding-housekeeper, having given her
evidence, asked leave to return to her muttons, as
“ by this time, my boarders will be clamouring
for their lunch.” “ The business of the country,”
retorted the rigid Bench, “ cannot wait on
boarders’ appetites.” That was better. An in-
stant’s inspection of the respective photographs of
Miss Nectarpull and Mrs. Gravyblub would fully
explain the motives of the Binch.

Gustave Sten, the Swede, arrested fur the
Domain murder, has been discharged from cus-
tody, there being against him really no more
evidence of murder than there is against the
newly-elected Primate. Nevertheless, the police
produced testimony to the effect that a knife
bearing blood-spots, with which the murder ap-
parently was committed, was exactly the sort of
knife used at the boarding-house where Sten and
the deceased lodged—the inference being, of
course, that Sten had brought it to the fatal spot;
also that Sten was seen to wash his clothes the
day after the murder —presumably to get rid of
the blood of the murdered man. Sten has had a
narrow escape—an escape which ought to make
the public shudder. Which of us may bo next?
The alderman who cuts his mouth with his knife
as he eats his dinner may be dropping on to his
shirt-front a blood-spot to be used as a seal for his
his own death-warrant.
■* # #
Among the ministers attending the General
Methodist Conference in Sydney was a “ super-
numerary ” (or retired) missionary who had once
supped with Cakoban just before the latter’s con-
version, and when cannibalism was still good form
in the courtly circles of Fiji. A most appetising
mess was prepared which gratified the nostrils of
the missionary as it was brought in, but alas ! dis-
turbed his conscience, for it did not require a
powerful imagination to conjure up the consti-
tuents of the dish. The King helped his guest to
the limb of a young and apparently tender —what
do you think? Chicken ! Sold again !
* # #
A clever, and therefore eccentric, Mel-
bourne gentleman residing at St. Kilda, and
equally well-known in legal and journalistic
circles, holds 23,000 Melbourne tram-shares, the
market price of which is now about £8 10s.
apiece. He is a bachelor, and hints that he means
to leave all his money to beautify Melbourne.
Someone lately suggested that he should proceed
to beautify it during his lifetime with a view to
seeing how it looked, but this view of the case
appeared to be rather distasteful to him. The
Bulletin recommends him to subsidise the Mel-
bourne City Council to an extent which will en-
able them to pump patchouli into the city gutters.

Here is a characteristic story. Two young
A 7 est Victorians, the hopeful scions of one'of the
wealthiest of Australian squatting families, not
long ago succeeded to a large fortune. They had
long been men about town and kings of the metro-
politan private bars. The first thing they did
was to rig up a private bar at their homesteads on
the station and procure the services thereat of two
yellow-haired beer-engineers. They couldn’t drink
anything unless it was served in the old familiar
way, doiicherknow.
* * #
Mr. Sidney De Montfort Bromley, until
now a stranger to the unprivileged portion of the
N.S.W. public, has just been appointed to the
deputy-governorship of Bernina Prison over the
heads of scores of deserving officers of the penal
department. Mr. Gould, Minister of Justice, is
alleged to be directly responsible for this little
transaction, but Mr. De Montfort Bromley’s
sponsors are said to be a military officer in high
command and a Supreme Court judge whose name
is hardly associated in the public mind with the
noblest ideals of British justice. Perhaps some
member of Parliament will kindly bore a hole in
i 18 Die Civil Service, and particularly
the officials of the prison service, yearn to know
what special virtues, apart from his name, are
possessed by Mr. Sidney De Montfort Bromley.
# #
The very latest from Manly Beach. A
gilded Sydney magnate now anchors his yacht a
couple of hundred yards or so from the public
landing-pier, and every night at nine o’clock fires
a cannon, which makes a terrific report—pre-
sumably just to let people know he’s there. The
common persons who are not allowed to shoot
within the municipality want to know why this
magnificent amateur navy - man is permitted
nightly to thus add another to the many curses of.
modern life. Should this kind of thing continue
much longer, some of the residents who have sick
people m their homes may fix up a Gatling-gmi
on the pier and answer the magnate’s blank-
cartridge.
* * #
A distinguished old gentleman resident in
a Sydney suburb lately made a terrible mistake.
He didn't feel well, had the influenza, perhaps,
and sent his son and heir to his office for the
letters. When the youth returned the old gentle-
man was not at home and so the correspondence
fell into the hands of his savage mother-in-law.
As some of the letters were very private indeed,
the result may safely be left to the imagination of
a wicked world.

Statistics have been compiled which go to
show that out of seven million young men in the
United States over five millions never darken a
church door—a piece of surpassingly powerful
testimony to the power of the sects !
“ Her Majesty the Queen lias unveiled

Some of the Sydney parsons are becoming
tame. The other day a sturdy little Wesleyan
clergyman with a big beard boldly sauntered down
Pitt-street with a lighted pipe in his mouth ; and
last week a priest looked in at a Sydney daily
newspaper-office and asked out the staff to the
nearest hotel where he. unblushingly took whisky
with them at the public bar.

A certain M.P., addressing the people of
a recently-flooded Australian township while the
waters were at their height, is stated to have said :
“I do not even know whether my wife and child
are in safety. 1 have remained among you to try
and save the town.” _ If true, rather rough on the
old woman and kid, isn't it?

Who shall say that society’s rotten,
And virtue’s not held in esteem

Is worked in the land of Our Gracious,
That pattern of true womanhood ?

To Albert the Good !

Awaken the multitude’s groan

How noble, how lofty, how great—
Is found in the work of this German
Whose offspring are fed by the State !

To Albert the Good.
The truth Tory toadies may garble,

Yet all royal sections in marble
Are doomed to be carted away.
They will go in a night, never leaving
A sign to denote where they stood;
For the masses have left off believing
That Alberts are good.

The Omaha Herald writes on the subject
we brought up lately, the article in the North
American Review, by Mrs. Gates, Brigham
Young’s daughter, on the beauties of Polygamy.
What is more significant, the t eminent is re-
printed by Public Opinion (London). Says the
Omaha man in other words : “ What’s the use of
declaiming against Polygamy, when this woman
challenges us to compare its supposed ends with
the awful real and tangible ones of Monogamy as
practised ?”

portion of Rockhampton cemetery was under
water while the R.C. corner was high and dry.

marked to a friend lying beside him : “Look at

# * *
The Supreme Court of Victoria has decided
that the word “ tiddleywink ” is not libellous.
Here goes, then : “ The funds of the Premier Per-
manent Building Society, of Melbourne, were
tiddleywinked by the directors.”
* * *
One of the Sydney daily papers tries to
make out that the police still don’t know who
shot Constable Slater. The Bulletin could tell
them in less than two tries.
# * *
One of the best things yet done by Mr.
Minister-of-Works Bruce Smith, who has done so
many good things and has refused to do so many
bad ones, is the abolition of the office of N.S.W.
Colonial Architect, with a view to the institution
of free competition among architects in general for
the designing of all public buildings over £lOOO in
value. Mr. Barnet, the late Colonial Architect,
has, it is true, designed some beautiful buildings,
but then, sad to say, he is directly or indirectly
responsible for the hideous Sydney Post-office
carvings, which are at last to go. The result of
the one-man system so far pursued is that Sydney
is a perfect Barnet-opolis.

Despite the precepts of their esteemed ex-
presideut, Mr. J. L. Purves, the average Austra-
lian Native seems capable of forming a tolerably
accurate estimate of British great men. Dr. Joyce,
a local medico, recently offered to deliver before
the Collingwood Branch of the A.N.A. a lecture
on Digestion or on W arren Hastings, as the
majority of the members might decide. The
Branch,* unhesitatingly plumped for Digestion.
Future A.N.A. lecturers might take a hint from
Joyce and ask intending audiences to make a
selection from such braces of subjects as : Pitt and
Pleurisy; Balfour and Bronchitis; Stanley and
Small-pox. #

There are signs that ere long a start will
be made with "the construction of a bridge to
Sydney’s Brooklyn—North Shore. That eminent
engineer, Norman Selfe, estimates the cost at
£200,000. Even with £lOO,OOO added for inci-
dentals the thing should pay splendidly.

There is no spot where thou and I together, for an horns

What gloomy and tragic secret lies behind these

A New Zealand provincial paper has made
a worse mess of things than usual by printing an
extract from one of Parkes’ poems, and crediting
it to an unknown bard with a plebeian name like
Jenkins, or Thompson, or something of the sort—
we forget the exact details. This display of ignor-
ance makes us feel suicidal and depressed. Unless
vigorous steps are taken future ages may credit
that poem to some 3001 b. son of song who carted
dirt in an obscure village somewhere, and Parkes
himself may go down the stream of history as a
beauteous maiden who was short of clothes in the
third century and was consumed in the Coliseum
in those stormy days when the tiger ate the
Christian and jumped on the Christian’s mother
with all four feet. History is mostly built that
way. It is constantly looking back into dim anti-
quity in search of ancient facts which, even when
they were new and fresh, wouldn't have kept one
family in boots for the winter. It is always want-
ing to know the circumstances which induced
some aged Chaldean parson to bang himself about
in the days of Belshazzar, or looking for the
evidence in the case of a venerable Sanscrit
individual who appeared for a fleeting moment
in history, and stood his wet umbrella up
against the throne of a forgotten king, and
was then ejected and vanished in the trackless ages
of B.C. It keeps hunting up the missing links
that connected something with a name like the
observation offered by a trodden-on cat with
something else that is as impalpable as the
shadow of a mule in a dense fog, and some day it-
will go hunting in the same unsuccessful way for
the great men who are living now. And how will
Parkes appear when that day comes ? Possibly
his name will be attached to a picture of a naked
Arab who broke his neck running races down a

granite precipice for a plug of tobacco. Perhaps
his memory will be preserved in a portrait that
looks as if a Carib had painted it with a fork at
midnight, and he will show up as a bow-legged
saint attired in a torn shirt and two immense
feet, and a smell of onions, tearing madly aero s a
desert with a manuscript hymn-book in his hand
and all his hair standing on end. Perhaps some
future generation may gather round a work of art
showing a stunted early Australian who lived in
a cave in an ag*e of slush and thunder and deluge,
and who ran round on all-fours, and neighed,
and ate grass like a horse, and calmly accept the
statement that he was one I'rancis Habigle, who
was ultimately caught by Confucius 'after a
desperate struggle in which that Chinese sage was
bitten in 16 places. And Dan. O'Connor, for all
he knows to the contrary, may bo alluded to at
some future day as an extinct animal that lived on
human flesh in the dark ages. So, after all, per-
haps it doesn't matter so very much if people do
begin to feel doubtful about who wrote larkes
poetry, seeing that sooner or later they will pio-
bably begin to get doubtful if there ever was any
Parkes at all. And even that won t matter so very
much either. In fact nothing matters very much
when we come to look at it closely, and, moreen er,
the world is feeling very tired and will be glad to
have a rest.
The incoherent cablegvaphic person who
supplies those items of foreign intelligence that
IVstor in the columns of the daily press would be
considerably improved if there were diagrams
attached to"him litre and there to explain what he
D thinking about. In Ids ordinary, nmllustrated
condition lie is too hazy to be much good He
■■ids down into the very bowels of a subject and
ferrets to come up again, and he has an exas-
iicratino' way of sending along the middle of an
event without telling who started it or explaining
where he got it so as to avoid being hanged, or
mentioning how the circumstance ended, or if it
didn't end at all but got exhausted and dropped
down dead on the way. For some weeks past he
has kept up an unceasing flow of items about the
French invasion of Dahomey, and has never once
thought of mentioning which party had got licked,
nor has he even told what the trouble is all about.
This last information was left to come along
slowly by delayed bullock-dray, and even when it
arrived it didn’t amount to much. As far as could
be made out, some unspecified nigger came along
to the French border and regarded the colonists
in a disrespectful manner with his eye, and
assumed an aspect which implied that the French
Republic made him feel sick, and having concluded
this demonstiation he left in a hostile manner and
couldn’t be found again. Then a deputation was
.sent to inform the shoeless, local potentate that if
this sort of thing happened again he would be
made to totter on his rhinoceros-hide throne,
and to demand that any person found snorting
or blowing or otherwise conducting himself m
a manner that reflected on the dignity of Europe
should be suppressed, but somehow the ambassa-
dors got mislaid and never returned. They were
received with reverence and lodged in a tent with
a skull on top of it. The royal umbrella, also with
a skull on top of it, was shown to them, and they
lived under the protection of the national banner
of Dahomey, which, like everything else m that
country, had a skull on top of it. The big imperial
drum, with skulls all round it, was pounded and
whaled andswalloped outside their tent until they
could hear themselves going deranged, and the
local orchestra, led by the principal executioner,
came along regularly and sang the hymn that the
old cow died of just when they wanted to go to
sleep Also they were honoured by the native
&d a »d tss
residence at midnight, and so did the ram-maker,
and the fetich-man, and the obi-man, and thegree-
gree-man, and the hangman, and the boss wizard,
and the principal snake-charmer, and the head
prophet, and the royal Latinist, and other ecclesias-
tics with nothing on ; and the palace dwarf lay in
the dust before their door and grosned all day
and bjew agonising yelps out of a trumpet in
between times. Also, the national snake-g d was
turned loose in their residence and couldn’t be
persuaded to go out; and, in addition to this,
a number of people were executed around
the ambassadors’ dwelling and their remains
tastefully stacked up in front; nd they were
visited by the royal family, which smelt like a
lost ash-barrel r ot.ing among a quantity of stale
hog; and the hospitable monarch offered his
visitors permission to kill anybody who they fan-
cied might be the better for being- execute* a
little, and gave them authonty to cripple the
bishop or wallop the royal harem when they
wanted exorcise. But he wouldn’t allow them to
leave, and consequently an army was sent to fetch
them back, and the cablegram lunatic began to
send along that series of expensive messages which
perpetually announce how the French troops had
started out from a heap of dust and marched till
they reached an extinct volcano, and blew up a
place that had been blown up all along, and laid
waste a spot that used to be a wilderness, and
camped one night in a dirty village of squalid
niggers, and the next in a squalid village of dirty
niggers, and then disappeared and hadn’t been
heard of since. And. this is what is called cable
intelligence! IntdWjcnrc above all things! Ix-

The Song of Old Joe Swallow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was up the country, in the days o’ long
ago,
Along ov Jimmy Nowlett with the teams I usetergo;
Then the reel road wasn’t heered on, an’ the-bush
was wild an’ strange, . '•
An’ we useter draw the timber from the saw-pits
in r,he range; .
An’ we useter load provisions for the stations, an
we’d go
Thro’ the plains an’ o’er the ranges in the days of
long ago. • t t
Chorus.- Then it’s yoke up the bullicks an
tramp beside ’em slow,
An’ saddle up yer horses an’ a-ridin’
we will go,
To the bullick-drivin’, cattle-drovin’
Nigger, digger, roarin’, rovin’
Days o’ long ago.
Once me and Jimmy Nowlett loaded timber for
the town,
But we hadn’t gone a dozen mile before the rains
come down,
An’ me an’ Jimmy Nowlett an’the bullicks an’
the dray
Was cut off on some risin’ ground while floods
around us lay;
An’ we soon run short of tucker an’ terbacca,
which was bad,
An’ pertaters dipped in honey was the only tuck
we had.
But it’s yoke up the bullicks, &o.
An’ half our bullicks perished when the drought
was on the land,
An’ the burnin’ heat that dazzles as it dances on
the sand —
When the sun-baked clay an’ gravel paves for
miles the burnin’ creeks,
An’ at ev’ry step yer travel there a rottin’
carcase reeks—
But we pulled ourselves together, for we never
used ter know
What a feather-bed was good for in those days o
long ago.
But in spite ov barren ridges an’ in spite ov mud
and heat,
An’ dust that browned the bushes when it rose
from bullicks’ feet,
An’ in spite ov cold an’ chilblains when the bush
was white with frost,
An’ in spite ov muddy water where the burning
plain was crossed,
An’ in spite of modern progress and in spite ov all
their blow,
’Twas a better land to live in in the days o long
ago.
Oft when the moon was shinin’ o’er the ranges like
a lamp,
An’ a lot of bulliok-drivers was a-campin’ on the
camp,
Wen the fire was blazin’ cheery an’ the pipes was
drawin’ well,
Then our s ngs we useter chorus an' our yarns we
useter tell;
An we’d talk ov lands we come from, and ov chaps
we useter know, _ 1
Bor there always was behind us other days o’ rong
ago.
Them early days was ended when the railroad
cro red the plain,
But in dreams I often tramp beside the bullick-
team 3 gain :
Still we pauses at the shanty just to have a drop
er cheer, . .
Still I feels a kind ov pleasure when the canipiu
ground is near:
Stilll smells the old tarpaulin me an’ J immy used
ter throw
O’er the timber-truck for shelter in the days o'
long ago.
Oh, I’ve been a driftin’ baok’ards with the changes
ov the land,
An’ if I spoke ter bullicks now they wouldn t
understand, .
But when Mary wakes me sudden in the night i'll
often say ; ,
“Come here, Spot, an’ stair up, Bally, blank an
blank an’ comeerway.”
An’ she tolls me when I m sleepin’ oft my eler-
quince ’illflow
In the bullick-drivin’language ov the days o' long-
ago.
The pub. will soon be closin’ so 111 give the thing
a rest, .
But if you drop on Nowlett in the far an' distant
west
If Jimmy drops a doubleyou instead of av an vee,
An’ if he drops his niches then yer sure to know
it’s he, .
