GRATEFUL ATHLETES.
'1 he following spontaneous expressions of
gratitude are taken from a recent issue of the
Manchester Athletic News
Mr. E. E. Painter (of London Athletic Club
and Ranelagh Harriers), 55 Finsboro’-road, South
Kensington, writes : “ I have been suffering from
a sprained tendon for the past three months, and
have tried every remedy I could think of, but
none have done me any good. About a month
ago I was recommended by a fellow athlete to
try St. Jacobs oil, which I applied at once, and it
did me so much good that I shall in a few day 3
commence running again. Shall recommend it to
all ray fellow athletes as being the best remedy I
have ever tried.”
Mr. G. Smith (of Hands worth Friary Football
Club), 18 Carlyle-road, Handsworth, Birming-
ham, says:—“Not long ago I received a severe
sprain in my ankle while on the football field,
wli ch wholly disabled me from walking. The
ankle was much swollen and very painful. Learn-
of the virtue of St, Jacobs oil in such cases, I pro-
cured a bottle and applied the contents regularly
for one week to the ankle, by which time the
swelling and pain had wholly disappeared, and I
was able to walk. I have also found the oil a
most excellent remedy for toothache and head,
ache. I have, therefore, no hesitation is recom-
mending the oil to football players, as well as all
persons suffering from pain.”
Mr. Thomas Charles Pullinger, the well-known
bicycle rider, of 16, High-street, Lewisham, not
only knows how to ride a bicycle, but he knows
the right sort of an embrocation to use after hard
riding. He says, in referring to a strained leg,
“I have found St. Jacobs oil has done my leg a
power of good. I shall continue to recommend
your valuable embrocation to my fellow cyclists,
as I consider it a splendid article for rubbing
down with while training. ” The Bicycling News,
April 23, 1887, also says : “Mr. Pullinger. of the
Catford, asks us to thank the gentleman who
recommended St. Jacobs oil to him.”
The fire brigade of the city of Birmingham has
reached an excellent state of efficiency. The men
are exposed to many dangers, and their arduous
work often brings about physical suffering. Chief
Engineer John Bellhouse, and firemen Josiah
Bennett, Thomas Fidoe, Win. Maeso, Wm. Shaw,
and John Brittill, write that they r ceived great
benefit from the use of St. Jacobs oil in eases of
severe pains in the back and knees also tender
feet and other ailments. The wives of several
members of the department also say that in their
case a few applications of the oil cured tooth and
face ache.
THE ENTERPRISING YANKEE.
To produce a watch so simple in construction
that it could be sold cheaper than any ether, and
still be a reliable timekeeper, has been the problem
for watchmakers the world over. The Swiss de
veloped the making of watches by hand to the
highest point, at the same time cheapening hand
labour to the utmost possible extent. The Ameri-
cans sought a solution in a different direction by
making the parts of their watches by machinery,
and interchange. However, the cost of manufac-
ture still remained too great for the watch of the
million. An enterprising Yankee company finally
solved the problem by dispensing with the greater
number of parts of a watch (reducing the number
from 160 to 57 !), making all of them by machinery,
and so easy of adjustment that expert labour was
not needed to put them together. This crowning
achievement in the history of watchmaking was
accomplished by the Waterbary Watch Company
in their Waterbury watch, which, though cheap,
is as perfect in its parts and as reliable in its time-
keeping as any watch made.
Series “E” —the best form of the original
Waterbury—is a good serviceable watch produced
at a cost within the reach of all. Keyless, reliable,
durable and accurate, it is without a peer for the
man or boy in moderate circumstances. Series
“J” (gent.’s size) and “L” (ladies’size) are the
latest productions of this famous factory. These
are short-winding, stem-setting, enamel-dialled,
jewelled and dust-proof watches, with duplex
escapements ; and are just as good timekeepers as
anyone could wish to carry. They have been
unanimously declared to be perfect marvels of
mechanical ingenuity. All these watches are
made by the best automatic machinery, and thus,
in event of accident, can be efficiently repaired at
a nominal cost, at any of the company’s repair
depots or agencies, established for the convenience
of wearers in various centres throughout the
colonies. The genuine Waterbury Watches may
be obtained from any respectable dealer or store-
keeper. Price, Series “E,” 13s. 6d.; Series “J ”
or “ L ” (enamel-dial), 225. Gd., (patent dial) 20s.
CAUTION.— AII Watches Manufacturen
BY the Waterbury Watch Go. have the word
“WATERBURY” printed in fine type upon
THEIR DIAL FACES, AND ALSO THE INTERWOVEN
LETTERS W.W.CO., WITHOUT WHICH NO WATCH
sold as A WATERBURY is genuine. If yon
want correct time and a watch which in event of
accident can be efficiently repaired at a reasonable !
cost, DEMAND THE WATERBURY.
Sandy, John, and Faddy.
[The following song was sung at the St. Patrick’s Day
Banquet, in London, by Mr. T. D. Sullivan, to the air of
“ So Early in the Morning.”]
The night is wearing fast away,
We see the dawning of the day ;
The “ flowing tide ” runs bright and free,
And bears our cause to victory.
Ten thousand thanks to Gladdy,
Say Sandy, John, and Paddy,
A braw and bonnie laddie,
A grand old guide is he.
He’s England’s pride, and Scotland’s joy,
Old Ireland loves the white-haired boy ;
His name is sweet through all the vales,
The hills and plains of gallant Wales.
Then hip hurrah for Gladdy,
Say Sandy, John, and Paddy,
A braw and bonnie laddie,
A grand old chief is he.
The toiling millions of our land
Got priceless blessings from his hand,
He knows the way, and has the will,
To make their fortunes brighter still.
Sing out the praise of Gladdy,
Say Sandy, John, and Paddy,
A braw and bonnie laddie,
A brave old man is he.
Full soon he’ll end the wrong and shame
To Ireland done in England’s name,
And make goodwill and peace to reign
Through all Britannia’s wide domain.
Our hearts go out to Gladdy,
Say Sandy, John, and Paddy,
A braw and bonnie laddie,
A lov’d old man is he.
God grant him strength, and length of days,
And bless his righteous works and ways,
And give him all his heart holds dear
To brightly crown his great career.
May God be good to Gladdy,
Say Sandy, John, and Paddy,
A braw and bonnie laddie,
A Grand Old Man is he.

Messrs. Chubb and Praeger, Beale’s
Chambers, have on view photos, of Mr. and Mrs.
Sydney Burdekin, the Mayor being in his official
costume. Both productions are of the highest
artistic merit, and reflect fresh lustre on these
already renowned photographers.
MERE FACTS.
The superior quality of the beer brewed by the
Anheuser Busch Brewing Association is the sole
reason that half a million barrels are sold anually.
This quantity would fill a sufficient number of
bottles to make a rosy girdle around the world, or
if put into kegs would fill enough waggons to
make an unbroken procession three hundred miles
long. Ye who quaff a glass of the famous St.
Louis Lager Beer, think of the good company
that you are in.
The Russian influenza, La Grippe, or by
whatever name our friends may choose to call it,
has paid us a visit in true earnest. It has been |
said that the first people to feel the influence of
the dread enemy are employees drawing fixed
salaries, and the first to get well are those whose
incomes depend upon their own exertions and vary
with the attention they devote to their business, j
This slight sarcasm may be true enough, but it is |
certain that there are many who have been really
ill, and the medical men and chemists have been
the gainers. Many of the prescriptions so freely I
published in the papers are merely what are known 1
as “placebos,” but there is no preventitive so I
efficacious as a good tonic. The grippe seizes i
those whose systems are weak and enfeebled by
age, overwork, or a too free enjoyment of the i
pleasures of life. To invigorate the system is to I
repel the subtle enemy, and in Banner's Tonic, j
which will be found duly advertised in our
columns, the public are provided with just the
medicine which is required. It strengthens the ;
weak constitution, repairs the ill effects of over-
work or debility, and is invaluable to those who j
have already become victims to the disease of the
age. i

THE REFEREE.
Another step in the aquatic championship
tournament was advanced on 15th May, when Kemp de-
feated SPLean. When M'Lean held Kemp for the first
200 yards or so, it began to look as if youth and strength
would pull M'Lean through, but old hands at the game
saw that the young 'uu was pulling the shorter and
faster stroke, and odds were called on the veteran.
Like most sculling it was decided in the
first mile. In the generality of cases a beaten man
takes a spell and then has another “spurt” at
his man, but the defeated sculler on Thursday
stuck to his work without an “ease up” from start to
finish. The tide was a perfect terror, running up the
river like a mill-race, the heavy swish, as it struck the
piles of the bridge, showing the pace it was travelling,
and, as the course was covered against it in 2lmin. 462 s <■•,
it may pretty safely be surmised that the scullers would
have broken the record had the tide been with them.
M'Lean has a deal to learn, but he has the years before
him to learn it in, so that it is not likely the last has been
heard of him in the championship world.
* * #
This Kemp-M‘Lean race, rowed against a strong
flood tide in fast time, showed the champion to greater
advantage as regards stamina than on prior occasions,
while M'Lean, who got flurried and dug up bottom at
every stroke, made little use of his long reach and strong
hack. The public now thinks better of Kemp than it has
ever done before, hut they do not lose sight of the fact
that in pulling M'Lean Kemp had the advantage of being
the more experienced practitioner, the old dog on a hard
road.
* # *
The Kemp-Stansbury people have certainly
managed matters very cleverly, as outside the “ stable”
it is not known which is the better man of the two.
O’Connor is the only man they have to fear, now M'Lean
is out of the way, and if Kemp be better than Stansbury
the “ stable” will know exactly what O’Connor is, taking
a line from his race with Stansbury before meeting Kemp
for the championship. Kemp’s offer to row O’Connor,
without waiting for the result of the Stansbury race, may
be only a hit of “ bluff,” but it looks very much as if his
people regard him as the better man.
# # #
Yes, the sculling championship muddle has so
far panned out to suit the combined “ gaffers’ push,”
though O’Connor may settle Stansbury and M'Lean.
Should Stansbury beat O'Connor, we may expect some
peculiar manoeuvres in connection with the championship.
Kemp’s easy victory on Thursday last shows what a dis-
appointing creature is the promising novice and “ coming
man ”of the sporting world. All of us can remember
scores of men, who were to beat creation, turning out
very common clay indeed when the critical moment came.
M'Lean’s form was truly foreshadowed by his race with
Chris. Keilson. The public may be a little too enthusiastic
.over that other promising man, Stansbury.
* * *
Not the least sensible feature of Beach's sculling
career was his selection of Peter Kemp for the succession”
apparently.
Haitian appears to be not quite played out yet.
At Bridgeport (C.S. A) he beat Plaisted in a mile spin.
#• # *
O’Connor’s training on the Parramatta strangely
recalls Hanlan’s first visit to England. The touts could
not reckon Hanlan up, as he was never extended, and the
long slide was new to them. We was matched against
Hawdon, and an unknown had challenged Klliott, the
champion, so people were much interested in the Canuck,
who, by the way, did his first spins on the Manchester
Canal, but soon removed to the Tyne. Elliott, disguised
as an amateur, attempted a feeler‘with Haitian one morn-
ing, but David Ward, of Toronto, who ruled his towns-
man with an iron hand, spoilt the little game.
#
Hawdon and Lumsdeu, two English scullers of
the past, are matched. The latter was on the decline
when Trickett first visited England. Hawdon, who is yet
a young man, may he called the last champion of
England. After a brilliant career this youthful sculler
was about to he matched against Elliott, when he was
challenged by the then despised Hanlan. Englishmen
then believed themselves invincible, but the way in which
the Canadian donkey-licked Hawdon first and Elliott after-
wards opened their eyes, for Trickett’s success was
regarded as an accident. After being beaten by Hanlan,
Hawdon retired, but he may yet turn out a fairly-decent
champion—for England.
# # *
The Champion Fours race at the N.S.W, Asso-
ciation Regatta on Saturday last proved a most amusing
affair for the spectators— before the atari : at the finish,
the Breakfast Creek (Q.) crew had the smile all to them-
selves. Three crews started—Queensland, Mercantile,
and Balmain. When the Queenslanders put out for their
preliminary they were treated to a loud guffaw by the
steamer-load of spectators, and 10 to 1 as to their chance
would not have tempted a response. Their boat, which
was built up North, compared to the Mercantiles, was as
an express-waggon to a piano-box buggy, and when
stroke began, his wriggle would have put to the blush a
hungry snake^ chasing his breakfast; but the steamer-
load grew serious whenever a start was made, as the
Queenslanders got away from the jump and won from start
to finish from the Mercantiles, who rowed a hopeless race
m splendid form. The style shown by the winners would
not put them in a maiden race so far as looking at them
is concerned, hut they get there all the same, and, pro-
perly boated, it may safely be said they would lose any
four New South Wales has yet turned out. C. B. P. Bell",
a thirteen-stoner, rowed for Cambridge for two years in
the Oxford-Cambridge race.
* # *
Jt is high time that the cable liar ceased gib-
bering reliable information concerning White’s horses in
England. Last Friday morning the Agk announced that
turkham was making rapid improvement, while Narellan
bad been thrown out of work. In the evening the little
Herald broke out into a rash statement that Narellan
'vas showing vast improvement on the training-track,
trom which Kirkham had been absent for several days.
In this conflict of ignorance the public can see, if they
) choose to, the absurdity of paying the least attention to
a “special correspondent’s” turf twaddle. In this case
each man gleaned his news from a different sporting
paper, and the tout of neither paper knows for certain
which horse is which as he glares at them from afar off
through a pair of field glasses. Touting in England
is a far greater farce than out here, where
it is a tolerably inane business, anyway. Should
this meet the eye of anyone who has owned or
trained a stud of horses “ at home ” he will endorse The
Bulletin’s solemn declaration herewith, viz., that the
sporting papers generally credit rattling gallops to the
horses which are in physic, and state that the nags which
come under their notice, morning after morning, have
not left the stable for a fortnight. When the ravings of a
tout, who can’t find out anything worth mentioning, are
filtered through a chuckle-headed correspondent who
knows nothing at all well, it’s best to pass. The
“opinions ” of Matthew Dawson may likewise be received
with silent contempt. It old Mat. spins yarns (which he
probably does not) concerning Mr. White’s horses, he does
“ the same with intent to deceive.” When he trained for
Lord Falmouth, at a time when the crack stable was
watched by an army of touts, he used to pull off the
classic events with rank outsiders from time to time.
Even “ Archer’s mount” would be allowed to start at 33
to 1, and win.

The first groat Australian racehorse to be
shipped for England was Moran’s steeplechaser, Dutch-
man, from Melbourne, a wonderful gallopper, that could
■jump like a kangaroo-dog, but Moran and horse had been
disqualified here, and it held good on t’other side, more
especially as Dutchman, with Mickey Moran up, would
have a throw-in for the Grand National.

One thousand pounds has been refused for Tito-
kowaru. We should like to see Tito, with owner up, and
both fit and well, start for the Liverpool (Eng.) Grand
National.
* * #
Harry Evans has effectually settled the preten-
sions of six Australian billiard-players, two of whom, at
least had friends ready to back them against the veteran
Britisher in an American tournament at Melbourne. The
Superiority of Evans in style, skill, and knowledge of the
game was apparent all the time, fully justifying what
The Bulletin has been saying of him since he descended
upon this hemisphere nearly eight years ago. After the
tournament a level 1000 up, “ spot-barred,” was played
by young Weiss and the champion. The latter calmly
waited for the N.S. Waler until they reached the final
lap, and then won. It is odd that Evans, after being out
here all these years, should be able to find fourth-raters
willing to play him on even terms without the “ spot,”
seeing that the old km’s London reputation was made “all
round the table.” We are sorry to give Evans away—but
he has a little more up his sleeve still.

Rome, and agree that it must have been fmr
millions, because the Circus Maximus was built
to hold 300,000, and neither women, children, noi

Thomson, the distinguished African travel
ler, author of “ Through Massai Land,” writes ii
truly awful style about the havoc which alcohol i:
wreaking amid the much-missionised African
savages. Three-fourths of the liquor, abominabk
rum, at Is. (3d. a gallon, is furnished by the
Germans ; even cheaper than the Christian whisky,
at ninepence a bottle, of which a thousand cases
lately went to Bombay, for the conversion of a
people who knew naught of it till glorious England
came.

afflict mankind.

Allcock’s Porous Plasters.
•—IN—
Chest, Liver, and Kidney Affections,
St. Yury an Vicarage, Grampound-road,
Cornwall, January, 18'JO.
Dear Sir, —I write to inform you that your
“ Allcock’s Porous Plasters ” arc excellent —a real
blessing. Whatever I may say, X feel I cannot
praise them sufficiently. I have given away
hundreds amongst the poor and suffering, and T
cannot recall a single instance where they' failed
to give relief. They have been used by me for
complaints in the chest, liver, kidneys, and spine,
with marked results, in fact, 1 know of three
special cases out of many (one of chest, one of
liver, one of kidneys) where the sufferers had been
to the infirmary, tried many doctors, without any
benefit, and wore told that nothing more could be
done, i gave them your plasters, and after a fair
trial of them the result was really wonderful.
The sufferer in the chest no longer complains of
weakness, has lost all distressing cough, and is
quite strong - . The sufferer from liver complaint
no longer complains of indigestion and pains in
the side and between the shoulders, but is quite a
different person. The sufferer from kidney com-
plaint no longer lies in bed nine months in the
year in agonies, but is able to get out of doors
every day, and is free from pain. I shall recom-
mend these plasters wherever I go.-—With all
good wishes for the New Year, yours very grate-
fully,
James Arthur Kemp, Minister.

The Queen Has a Cold!!!— Cablegram.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“The Queen has a cold !”
Fite fact I’d forgotten as soon as ’twas told,
It Caused mo at first neither sorrow nor gladness,
Though twas told in a tone of most exquisite sad-
ness—
“ The Queen lias a cold I”
. “ The Queen has a cold !”
Cood gracious ! well, now if I rniytd make so bold
As to state that my life become- none the less
cheerful.
Though you moan once again, as I'm not looking
tearful,
"The Qutm has a cold !”
“ The Queen has a gold ! ”
I’heK talk of nought else, do my friends young and
old : " ,
As 1 fail to see cause for solicitude in it
I’ll he saying rude things of the Queen in a
minute—
The Queeii with a cold.
•“ The Queen has a cold ! ”
Well, I wonder that all of you haven’t got hold
Of more details connected with Royalty’s wheezing,
When the Court is a-coughing, the Sovereign
sneezing,
The Queen has a cold.
“ The Queen has a cold !”
Once Was too often, but still they unfold
The sad story—myself I’ll absent for a little
Until as all others this craze prove as brittle,
This “ Queen with a cold,"’
(After an absence of several months.)
The year’s growing old,
Trees in the park shower crimson and gold,
1 expected a welcome, but got, as a greeting,
The familiar drivel, the annual bleating :
“ The Queen has a cold ! ! "

A Bundaberg (Q.) journalist, named John
Rowland, has invented an apparatus for cutting and
topping cane in the field, if it proves successful,
the chief excuse for the employment of kanaka
labour is doomed.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contrllmtors
Strictly Original matter contributed to This Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive Mid
I consider contributions as under :—
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
U. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Silk Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves ■ responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped, and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

“ Rockhampton ” : Barry’s case is as bad an instance of
savage brutality as we hav e ever heard of; we much
doubt whether the man could have been both sane and
sober. If the judge, as reported, really told the jury that
it was their duty to find the prisoner guilty of murder,
then the prisoner should be reprieved and the judge
should be fired right off the bench in one act. Not more
than one province can afford to tolerate Mount-Ren-
nieism. [Many answers held over.]
Judging from the recent cable about
“Queen’s Birthday ‘honors,’” the Imperial
Government mean to try and politically bribe with
titles a number of members of the Australian
Federal Convention.
Editor Bulletin, — In the early days of
the Palmer goldfield, carriage from Cooktown to
the “field” being extremely heavy, goods were
proportionately high—so high, in fact, that many
a poor devil had to curtail his supply of necessaries
and leave luxuries severely alone. It is on record
that one storekeeper sold to a customer a “packing-
needle ” for the moderate sum of half-a-crown—
a price to which the purchaser demurred in the
language peculiar to pick-and-shoyel men. How-
ever, he was much mollified and paid for the
article without demur when the vendor vociferated,
“ Arrah, look at the carriage I paid on it!”
A Sydney evening paper says that the man
arrested on suspicion for the Domain murder
“wore a look of foreign doggedness” as he
appeared at the inquest. Such comments on a
man whose life is at stake are not only indecent,
but worthless. The sapient Sydney press put the
victim down as a regular Domain loafer, whereas
he was actually a hard-working, thrifty, prosperous
and well-nourished man.
An elegy in the obituary column of a Mel-
bourne newspaper begins thus :
His little triangle, for months he played,
In the South Melbourne Army Band:
But he’s left his place in the army below
For a place on the golden strand.
We suppose that by this time the deceased saints
of the Salvation Army have it all their own way
on the golden strand, and the old celestial per-
formers, with their one tune, da eapo everlastingly,
liave had to come down to playing overtures for
<i reuses in the country parts of Heaven. This
suggests to us a little apologue in verse :
A sweet soul was (say) William Shand,
But very nervous;
He hated the Salvation Band
As some men Purves.
And when he died and found that band
Cawhooping madly
In Heaven, he took St. Peter’s hand,
And whispered sadly ;
“ I’ve been deceived—it makes me sick —
This is a sell, sir,
If this be Heaven, pray show me quick
The road to hell, sir.”

The Cooktown (Q.) Independent on local
furies: —
For five years there have been selected in this town as
intelligent jurors, some ignorant and criminal blackguards,
who can’t write their own names and never learned to
even read the Lord’s Prayer. Several o£ the selected,
on whose opinions men’s liberties and lives depend, are
drunkards, thieves, wife-beaters, and coarse libertines,
and yet a dignified judge and his flippant associate tell
the criminal the legal rubbish about “ Whose country jou
are,” and the solemn farce goes on evidently tor yews.
On one empanelled jury in Cooktown during the last -
months there were men severally sworn, who had been
convicted of that most cowardly of all social offences, viz.,
beating to jellies the poor trusting women who became
their wives.
A recent anecdote of Tennyson reports him
to have uttered, in the course of a country walk
“ What an imagination (ton has ! and a British
bishop goes into ecstasies over the phrase, fcut it
makes one almost despair of the intellectual
advancement of the race that a man like Tenny-
son should at this time of day conceive of the
Deity as being a reflex of petty Inimanity, W hen
4 4 good people ” read their virtues into God, and
thinking and imaginative people their mental
qualities into Him, they mustn't be surprised if
the bad and sinful sort see in Him their qualities
also. An anthropomorphic God lias got to be
human all round.
Dear Bulletin, —I see you have your weather-
eve on these parts. There are people, and people belong-
ing to one of the Six Families, who if they don’t directly
advocate the employment of Chinese labour on these tin-
fields do so by implication. In the report of a meeting
held in Bunhury, the other day, Sir J. Lee Steere (the
man who is credited by his enemies with ha\ mg said If
a man be born poor ho should remain poor”) said that
the Tasmanian tin-fields never proved a success till the
advent of cheap Chinese labour. Now the tin-fields from
which I write are anything but a success, with the excep-
tion of about half-a-dozen claims, the failure of

the place being in a great measure due to the
fact that the present leasing-system contains a labour
clause unworthy of the name, and any outsider would
very naturally assume that Sir J. Lee Steere would
like to try the remedy that he imagines acted well in Tas-
mania. This is, indeed, a nice return to make after the
help given by the Eastern provinces to W.A. re the Re-
sponsible Government question, for Sir J. Lee Steere
knows perfectly well that the other Austrahas are dead
against the introduction of Chinamen. A batch of Chows
were imported only a few weeks ago by contractors, but
not just about here. Somebody has nicknamed Sir J. Lee
Steere “ the Longitudinal Steere.” —Yours truly, Sluice-

Dear Bulletin, — Affiliation of the Sydney
Institute of Journalists and the \ ictorian Re-
porters’ Association is on the carpet. The former
is almost on its legs, while the latter ik going
ahead by leaps and bounds. 4’he men who have
been most active in the former would be excluded
under Victorian rules. Therefore, if the move-
ment is to boconic intercolonial, the Institute had
better admit only reporters in future, and it is the
duty of reporters to see that this is done. In time,
if some energy, sadly lacking at present, be
show'n, the Institute will consist of reporters only,
and then affiliation will serve a most useful pur-
pose and will hurry up Adelaide and Brisbane.
The V.R.A. emphatically declines to admit any
pressmen but reporters even as honorary members.
Can’t some of the old Melbourne men now
reporters in Sydney entirely remodel the institute ?
It must come in time. Ere long there will be
associations on the wholesome lines of the V.R.A.
in all the-colonies, and the offices which men only
enter when they are, hungry and which they always
leave with a curse on their lips will have to look
round. The V.R.A. yearns for Sydney, but that
Institute is up to now unworkable.—A V ictorian
Reporter.
Henry Hyde Champion, who it is asserted,
is about to visit Australia, is the son of a Major-
General in the British army. Young Champion, a
mere lad at the time, resigned his lieutenancy
because of his sense of the injustice of the British
occupation of Egypt. His father having with-
drawn his support from him on that account and
partly because he had gone over to Socialism with
R. B. P. Frost (Mrs. Gordon Baillie's last pro-
tector, who was entrapped by her during an in-
terview for the P.M. Gazette in Edinburgh) the
ex officer invested las cash with Fuulgcr in the
Modern Press, and among other publications
brought out Justice, then backed up by the. cash
and contributions of Hyndman and Carpenter.
The venture failed, however, and Champion, who
had been notorious for his recommendation ot
■armed"revolt,'convinced by the Trafalzar-square
dispersion that an undisciplined rabble had little
show with armed and drilled forces, however
small, seemed to lose heart and gradually fell out
of touch with Socialistic and Radical thought.
His wife, a refined and delicate woman, died trom
the shock while he was being tried for participa-
tion in the “Square riots,’ then followed the
arrest of his friend, Frost, for whoso defence
Champion and Mai tin an Barry found the funds.
Champion is but little over thirty years ot age, is
a good speaker, and, it is said, of late years has
become possessed of considerable sums of money.
The Sydney Morning Herald endeavours to dis-
count the distress existing in New South Wales
and show the evils of Protection by pointing out
that “of 217 men who slept one night lately in
the drill-shed at Dawes Point (13 were from Vic ?
torn, 33 from Queensland, 24 from New Zealand,
10 from South Australia, 2 from Tasmania, 9 from
America, 2 from Germany, 1 from Italy, 20 from
England, 23 from Ireland, 5 from Scotland, and
25 from New South Wales. Of the 25 who repre-
sented themselves as belonging to New South
Wales 20 were recognised by the police as habitual
park-loafers. ” “ Were from, ”we presume, means
that the individuals thus classified were born in
the spot alluded to. Thus, according to the
Herald, a man not born in New South Wales
never attains its full rights of citizenship, and no
matter what his condition the fiscal law's or social
environments of that colony can never be deemed
responsible for it. A man may have left England,
Germany, or Victoria 50 years ago to settle down
in New South Wales ; but, no matter—the country
ho originally hailed from is responsible for ail his
his miseries, and not his adopted home. T his is a
fair sample of what passes editorial muster as logic.
The Welsh are strongly opposed to capital
punishment. When a man was executed at Dol-
!r,.]|y some years ago the authorities had to import
the gallows’ timber as well as the hangman.
An Italian giant, who stood over seven feet
high and was proportionally stout, has recently died.
Before 11 pegging out ” he sold his skeleton to the Anatomi-
cal Museum, in Home, for 10,000 francs.

Marjorie’s Fortune.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I. don’t mind confessing it. I felt half-way
in love with Belle Viney at first sight : she was so
entirely different from all the other women I had
met. But she got engaged to another fellow and
I had to give her up. I had had considerable
experience in affairs of the heart, for I was the
possessor of seven girl cousins, each of whom, I
am hound to say, was constantly discovering some
charming friend or friends to whom “Cousin
Tom ” really must be introduced. But if Master
Tom Nugent was from time to time enslaved by
any of Cupid’s footpads, his chains were of the
lightest, and broke after a very brief spell of
captivity.
So my twenty-fifth birthday found me still
heart-whole, with no despotic She to decide for
me where 1 should spend my coming holiday. It
wanted but a week to the time, I was to have a
good solid month, but up to that moment I hadn’t
a notion where I was going to spend it. My desires
were large but my screw was small, and much as
I had been looking forward to a Melbourne'trip*
1 was obliged to confess that I must give it up,
and be content with a spell of country life nearer
home.
“I wish I knew of some jolly, quiet place,” I
said to my office-chum, Dick Egan ; “ some
house near the sea, where there are no other
hoarders, where a fellow eonld swing in a ham-
mock and read and smoke and dream the days
away to his heart’s content: some place where a
fellow could get fresh milk and eggs and lots of
fruit, and a tender chicken now and then. By
Jove, if I could hit upon a place like that I think I
could manage to put in a month easily enough ;
and it wouldn’t cost much.”
“Advertise,” quoth Dick, sagely; “all things
come to him who advertises.”
Before 1 could either question or corroborate
Dick’s airy bit of philosophy, little Billy Hawker,
our 14-year-old messenger-boy, looked up from a
parcel he was tying and said, wbh a twinkle in his
bright eyes—
“ Perhaps, sir, you’d like to come down to our
place; I think our folks would have you. We’ve
got a nice bit of farm and lots of chickens and eggs
and things.”
“Why, Hawker,” said I, “ I didn’t know you
had any folks. Where’s the place?”
“Down Penrith way, sir,” he answered ; “I’ve
often told father that you’re very kind to me, and
I think he’d like you to eome.”
“ I say, Dick, this is worth looking after.
Dashed if I don’t think it’s the very thing I
want. ”
“ Sure to be something against it,” was Dick’s
sotto voce, unfeeling reply; “ like the ads. of the
plain cooks, strict teetotallers, and the hash-
houses, with every home comfort.”
“Well, I’ll go and see for myself,” said I.
“I’ll go down this very afternoon (which was
Saturday), and you’d better come, too, Billy.”
So that afternoon at four o’clock found Billy
Hawker and me at the Penrith station, and after
a couple of miles’ walk we found ourselves at the
gate of a good-sized, well-built house with a garden
gay with flowers.
“Here we are, sir,” cried Billy, “and there’s
Marjorie—she's my sister, sir—feeding the hens.”
Looking to where he pointed as we entered the
gate I saw the prettiest girl my eyes had ever
rested on. She was holding the corner of a large
white apron in one hand, while from it she dis-
pensed the chickens’ evening meal with the other.
“ Great Scott!” thought I to myself, ‘it was
worth the trip just to get a look at yon, my
beauty.”
As we went in, the girl came forward, her eyes
dancing with delight at the sight of her brother.
Somehow we didn’t seem to need much intro-
duction.
“This is Mr. Nugent,” said Billy, “come to
see if mother’ll take him for a month's board.
“ Pleased to see yon, sir,” said Miss Hawker.
“ Billy has often told ns how kind you are to him.
We went into the house, and after 1 had seen
and talked with the old people I made up my
mind that I was in the right crib to spend my
holiday. They offered me a small, nicely-fur-
nished room facing some grand mountains, and
said I was not to think of going back to Sydney
that night.
“ You’ll be able to tell on Monday whether you
like the place or not,” said Mrs. Hawker.
“ Like the place ! ” It did not take me long to
make up my mind about that. As I settled to
sleep that night it seemed just heavenly, after the
dust and noise and stuffiness of my second floor
back in Phillip-street. And in the morning the
eggs and milk and scones and honey were just
heavenly, too.
Sunday passed all too soon, and I left next
morning for one more week’s grind at the mill
before 1 could once more turn my step to their
newly-discovered Paradise.
Of course Dick Fgan wanted to know all about
it.
“ Oh ! it’s a very pretty place,” I told him :
“ very quiet,” and I proceeded to enlighten him
on the further attractions of the place. Did I
mention the fair Marjorie ? Well, at this distance
of time I can scarcely remember such an unim-
portant detail ; but I rather fancy I didn’t.
“ Say, old man,” said Dick, suddenly, “ when
you’ve come back I've half a mind to rim down
there myself. If it’s all yon say it is it must be
just the place I should like ”
“Ah, well,” replied 1, hesitatingly, but not
without emphasis, “ I don’t think it would suit
you a bit, Dick. Why, my dear fellow, you’ll get
the bines there in two-twos ; it would be much too
dull for you ; you want some fun. / only want
a pipe and a book, and there I am !"
* # *
Saturday was a long time coining, but,
like other Saturdays it came at last, and the
evening found me installed in the cozy room at
the farm. Need I say that long before the end
of the next week, to put it mildly, I was head over
ears in love with Marjorie?
Ah ! she was a bonnie country lass if ever there
was one —frank, bright, and unaffected, and with
no faults that I could see. The only weakness, if
such it can be called, which I could discover was
a certain tendency to superstitions in little mat-
ters. If she spilt any salt she would instantly
throw a few grains over her left shoulder ; she
wouldn't for the world start away from home
on a Friday; on no account must an umbrella
or parachute be opened inside the house ;
such an act, it was her belief, would be
quickly followed by some terrible misfortune.
A magpie crossing her path would quite upset her
mentally. These and other little matters of a
like kind were as so many articles of Marjorie’s
creed. At first I used to laugh at her, and tried to
reason with her about the absurdity of the thing,
but when I saw' she didn’t like it I quickly left off
that, and humoured her in every possible way.
Yes, I fell in love with Marjorie. But there was
one bitter drop in my cup ; Marjorie did not fall
in love with me. She liked me, but I was certain
she never thought of me as a lover. I passed a
great deal of my time with her; read to her,
looked alter the flowers with her, twisted the
chickens’ necks for her, helped her in all sorts of
ways, but she gave me no sign of love. The last
day of my holiday came, and I bid good-bye
with a queer feeling in my heart, as if the sun
would never shine again till I went back there.
* * #
Weeks passed, and I heard nothing from
my kind friends till one morning Bill, who had
just come back from his holiday, came to me and
said:
“I’ve got a note for yon, sir, from Marjorie”
(oh, how my heart jumped !). “She said it was
very partic’lar, and I was to be sure to give it into
your own hands.”
I opened the note, from which a little packet of
tissue-paper fell out, and read as follows ;
Bear Mr. Nugent,- —I send yon a lock of my hair (my
heart here fairly galloped), and 1 would be so much
obliged if yon would take it to Madame R., 798 Surry-
hills, and ask her to burn it and tell me my fortune from
the ashes. She told Phoebe Raven’s in that way, and it's
come so true 1 want mine as well. Phoebe is to be married
on the Ist of May, and we want you to come to the wed-
ding. Please excuse me troubling you, but you are the
only gentleman friend 1 have in Sydney, and 1 don’t want
Madame R. to know whose hair it is. Bon’t laugh at me,
and believe me Yours truly,
Marjorie Hawker.
P,S. —Be sure and come to the wedding.
For a moment or two I really believe my impulse
was to throw the packet out of the window. It
seemed such a silly commission from a sensible
girl. But as I opened the packet and took out
the long bright tress of hair, and kissed it, and
thought of all Marjorie’s grace and charm, I re-
solved to do her bidding.
But in that moment of traitorous indecision, a
hideous and overwhelming temptation came upon
me, an seized me, and made me its prey. An
idea flashed into rny mind, which I lost no time in
putting into action on the time-honoured and
well-worn principle that all is fair in love and war.
That afterno n, when Dick Fgan came saunter-
ing b«ek from his lunch, I said
“ Dick, do you think you could write out a full,
true and particular description of your humble
servant? ”
“What the define do you mean, Tom? ”
“What I say, Mr. Richard Fgan. Do you
feel capable of writing out, nothing extenuating,
setting down nought in malice, a full, true,
and particular description of this child, Thomas
Nugent? ”
“Certainly, Tom, if yon wish it I’m on. And
I’m not to flatter you ? ”
“Not a bit; lean a little to the side of mercy,
but do it so that your conscience will never re-
proach you hereafter ; and mention no names.”
“All right, here goes. ” And at the end of ten
minutes Dick handed me this description of my-
self.
He is five and twenty ; medium height and well-made
blue eyes; bright brown hair, inclining to curl: straight l
nose: rather large but well-formed mouth, partly hidden
by moustache: mole near left eyebrow; small hands:
bright smile ; looks best when he’s in a good temper : is
fond of poetry and music, and good things to eat: quick
tempered, hut soon gets over it : hates work, but never
shirks it; kind to animals : rather shy : altogether a good
fellow.
“ Will that do?” asked Dick.
“ Well, it’s a little ”
“No it isn’t, not a bit; and I shan’t alter a
word.”
Well, I couldn’t alter it without betraying my-
self. So 1 put it into an envelope, together with
some mysterious-looking hieroglyphics (of anything
but Oriental origin;, and sealing it with a la"ge
seal, enclosed with the whole a note as follow's;
My dear Miss Hawker,—
I send you what .you ask. I hope your fortune may
prove satisfactory. I will he at the wedding if possible.
With kind remembrance to your father and mother, I am.
yours most faithfully,
Thomas Nparnt.
I went to Phoebe Raven’s wedding. When it
was over, Marjorie and I went for a stroll, in the
course of which she suddenly turned to me and
said
“Oh ! Mr. Nugent, I'm so much obliged for the
trouble you tooi about Madame R. 1 dare say
you thought me foolish, but Phoebe’s fortune came
so true : she’s married just the man she was told
she would.”
“ And is not yonr's likely to be true as w r ell ?” I
asked.
“No-o-o, because —you see—well, she described
a person as my— my—who—well—he’s been a long
time somebody else’s.”
“ How do yon know that, Marj—Miss Hawker,
may I ask ?”
“Oh ! ■ Billy told me I mean —O, Mr.
Nugent ”
Then she actually broke down and began to cry,
and in her tears (somehow 7 her head was now on
my shoulder) /die told me the happiest story I had
ever listened to.
1 clasped her in my arms; “Billy said what,
my darling?”
“He said you were engaged to a beautiful
young lady who went to England the other day,
and you’re going to marry her when she comes
back.”
“ Marjorie, that beautiful young lady is my
cousin, Belle Viney, and she has gone to England
to be married. Master Billy has been mixing
things a little.”
“ Oh ! then, Tom—Mr. Nugent—it’s all right,
and I’m so happy; she described yon exactly. ‘
A. W.

TH E PROFESSOR'S DISCOVERY:
A Tale of Old Ravenswood.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“The transmutation of metals,' said the Pro-
fessor, as he seated himself on the end of bams
bunk, just inside the tent, “is not devoid or
achievement; to a certain extent I have, 1 con-
sider, eliminated the discovery.’' Ihe Professor
always went in for high-sounding words, but
somehow he did not always fit them into tue right
place.
It was on Pa veils wood diggings, in -N orth
( fueensland, but alas ! after its best days had tied.
We were all of us hard-up, and, at the time the
Professor appeared on the scene, Sam and 1 were
thinking of deserting it for fresh fields and pas-
tures new. The Professor—“ Professor Marcias ”
he called himself—had come up on the field with a
very modest show of legerdemain, but the held
had as much as it could do to find itself in tucker.
There was no money to pay for amusements, so
the show was a frost, and the Professor pottered
about in an abandoned claim just below ours, and
used to come up at night and give us the benefit
of his company and conversation. Ho was a
little, dark man, with a Spanish or Mexican look
about him, but he spoke stilted English without
any accent and was very reticent about his past,
“ So you think you have discovered the philoso-
pher’s stone, do you ?” said Sam.
“ Yes, I think I may claim to an abstract know-
ledge of the science "f transmutation,’ said the
Professor. , , , ,
“ Well,” I said from my bunk, 1 think you
might try your hand on some of the mullock about
here.”
“ Ah, it is too costly, it is out of my power just
now.” ... , .
“Oh, I see," said Sam, “takes five pounds to
make an ounce of gold.”
“ Is there nothing else of a cheaper sort we can
try ?” I asked.
“There is, there is the divining-rod. Now,
with that I am an adept, and on Sunday we will
give it a trial.”
“ What will it find ?” said Sam.
“Find? It will find water, quicksilver, pre-
cious stones, and gold and silver.
“ That’s pretty well for one contract. Why
didn’t you try it before ?” remarked Sam.
“What was the good?” said the Professor
eagerly; “it may indicate to me the existence of
much wealth, but I have no money to follow up
the indication —it costs much to sink a shaft.’
“ How did you learn it? ” I asked.
“ An Indian chieftain in South America taught
me, and we found large treasures together.
“ The deuce you did ! And what became of
them, pray? You don’t seem to have kept much?”
“No! I was robbed and left penniless in San
Francisco. Then I made my way over to Aus-
tralia as —cook’s mate, and came up here in hope
uf getting luck once more.”
“Well, Professor, seeing that the experiment
won’t cost anything, we don’t mind giving you a
show next Sunday.”
It was on Tuesday that we made the promise and
we had almost forgotten it, but the professor had
not, and on Sunday he presented himself at our
tent and reminded ‘us that it was the day of his
performance.
The Professor informed us that he had had
much trouble in finding a stick of the right kind
of wood, but had at last got one that he thought
would serve for minerals, but he could not pro*
mise that it would indicate water. So, m company
with some more of the boys, whom we had asked
to take the thing seriously, we went with the rro-
fessor a short distance away. Ihe little man pro-
duced his stick, a forked one, and holding it by
one of the forks in front of him, proceeded with
the utmost gravity to trot about. For some time
he had apparently no success, but at last, wnetnei
by design or not, his rod was strongly attracted
downwards, and he remained stationary. Wo
sauntered up and asked him what he had found.
The little fellow was as white as a sheet and he
seemed greatly agitated. “I do not know, he
said, “ I have such a strange feeling. It is not
gold nor silver nor water, lam frightened ; what
can it be ?” . . ~, ,
“Well so far us 1 can make out, it s an old hole
that has caved in, and filled up last wet season,
said Sam. “Try somewhere else, old boy, im
I afraid you haven’t struck gold this time.
“Yes, old guy,” said a rude, rough voice,
6 i take your d d play-acting elsewhere .
We all looked up. A dark man, with an inso-
lent laugh on his face, was standing near. Me was
a stranger to all of us, and stood looking on with a
most aggravating sneer. Marcias recovered him-
self at the voice, and walked on a little further,
but the jest had gone out of it all. Sam lookec
pretty keenly at the stranger, but said nothing,
and as by this time nearly half the scanty popula-
tion had assembled, the man turned oft with
another sneer.
“ Who is he ?” said the sergeant of police, who
was standing near me.
“ I don’t know,” I replied “not a very agreeable
sort of fellow by his looks.”
“ He’s stopping at Martin's,” said a man near
me, naming one of the two still-existing pubhc-
houses, “ I saw him there last night-says he was
here about six months ago. '
“ Hum. I wasn’t here then,” said the sergeant.
“ What are you up to with the Professor ?'
“ He’s experimenting with the divming-rod. He
says there’s something in this old hole, he can t
teil what,” I answered.
“ Marcias,” said Sam, “ come and have another
The Professor came up and walked over the old
filled-up hole, and once more was affected in cue
same manner. , , ,
“ At it again ! ” said the stranger, who had re-
turned, “ this hole’s my property. Met off it,
blast you ’’—and he gave the little man a violent
Pl “*Let him alone ! ” cried half-a-dozen voices in
such tones that the fellow drew back.
“What’s up, Professor?” said Sam ; tell us
tell,” said Marcias, who was white to
the lips, “it is not gold, nor silver, nor water.
Ah ! my God ! it is murder !” and he would have
fallen but that Sam kept him up.
“ This is a queer sort of Sunday-play, said tne
stranger; “ have you quite done !
“ What is it to yon, I should like to know,
whether we’ve done or not ? ” said the sergeant,
who was not in uniform. , .
“ A good deal,” said the other ; this was my
claim when I was here before, and I’ll trouble you
to move off it. Ain't I right, Martin? he said,
turning to the publican, who now made one ot tne
crowd. . , e
“ It was your claim when you were here bet ore,
but I don’t know the law of the thing—better ask
the sergeant.”
“So you’re the sergeant, are you ? said the
stranger : “ well, I think if you looked after your
business you’d send these pfeople home.
“ 111 look after my business, my man, and that s
such fellows as you,” said the Other, sternly.
“Hunter,” he whispered to me, take two or
three fellows and get your tools. We 11 soon see
what the hole is stuffed with. Either this fellow
is playing the fool or he’s mighty anxious that ne
shan’t do it.”
When I came back I found the stranger appeal-
ing to the crowd. “ Look here !” he said, sup*
posing a fellow left a pocket behind him against
he came back, hard-up, don’t you think he has a
right to it ? I left a little bit of good stuff there
before last wet season, and I’m sure there s nobody
here would want to take it from me. ’
“You shall have all the gold we find down
here,” said the sergeant— “meantime, for your own
sake, say no more,” he added, meaningly.
“You chatter-headed little varmin, said the
stranger, turning fiercely on Marcias, but bam
stepped in between them. Meanwhile some of us
fell to work. The stranger turned away. • Well,
I’ll leave you to such sport,’ he said; but the
loud voice of the sergeant called to him to stop.
The man turned round defiantly and then walked
on again. The sergeant blew his whistle, and the
policeman who was on duty at the barracks came
down the ridge. When I went up to get the
tools. I had brought down my revolver, and slipped,
it into the sergeant’s hands. .
“ Stop !” he called after the man again, who now
saw that he was hemmed in between the two.
“ Yon don’t leave this place until we ve got to
the bottom of the hole.” The fellow turned back
with an insolent air, but he was evidently uneasy
beneath it all.
The work went on merrily, and, as the hole mid
never been very deep, it was soon cleaned out.
“ We’re down on the rock," said one.
“It’s here,” muttered the Professor, pointing
down to his feet. . ,
“See if there’s not a drive, said the sergeant.
“Yes; here’s a filled-up drive,” returned the
man after a bit, and he fell to work on the face of
it. Soon he started up excitedly. Meres a
man’s boots !” he cried.
“ Go on as quick as you can ; you'll tmd a man
in them, I expect,” and he and his subordinate
closed in on the man who had so hopelessly given
himself away. . ,
It did not take long to unearth the remains and
bring them to the surface, they were unre-
cognisable, but the man had evidently been
murdered—the back of his head had been stove m
with a pick. , , ~ ■
The man who was arrested, contessed to tuc
crime—the murder of his mate. What uneasy
sting of conscience led him back to the spot and
caused him to make himself conspicuous, Heaven
only knows. _ . • ~ ~ r
“ Look here, Marcias,” I said to the Professor,
“ did you know anything about that body ? Gome
now, explain all that hanky-panky. ’
“ I know nothing,” said the little man vehem-
ently. “ I know this now, that that man was the
same who robbed me in San Francisco, and i
think my thirst for vengeance made me guess his
secret unwittingly, but I tell you the truth when I
say I did not act at all.” , ,
I never saw the Professor after he left, but we
raised him a small subscription and started him on
his way rejoicing.
DELCOMYN.
The Glass on the Bar.
(HENRY LAWSON IN “MUDGEE INDEPENDENT.”)
Three bushmen one morning rode up to an inn,
And called for their glasses of whisky and gm ;
They’d only returned from a trip to the Worth,
And linger to greet them the landlord came forth.
He absently poured out a glass of “ three-star,”
And set down that drink with the rest on the bar.
“ There,, Unit is for Harry,” he said, “an’ it's
’Tis the very same glass that he drank from last
His 'name’s on the glass, you can read it like
He scratched it himself with a sharp piece of Hint.
I remember his drink, it was always * three
And the landlord looked out through the door of
the bar.
He looked at the horses and counted but three.
“You were always together, wheres Harry/
cried he.
Oh, sadlv they gazed at the glass as they sam—
“ You may put it away, for our old mate is dead.
But one, gazing out o’er the ridges afar,
Said, “ We owe him a shout ; leave the glass on
the bar 1”
They thought of the far-away grave on the plain,
They thought of the comrade who earae not again ;
They lifted their glasses and sadly they said,
“ We drink to the name of the mate who is dead .
And the sunlight streamed in, and a light like a
Seemed to glow in the depth of the glass on the
bar.
And still in that shanty a tumbler is seen.
It stands by the clock, ever polished and clean,
And often the strangers will read as they pass
The name of a bushman engraved on the glass.
And tho’ on the shelf but a dozen there are,
That glass never stands with the rest on the bar.
When the guides get really hard-up for a
show-place in the N.Z. Hot Lakes district they
lug the open-mouthed tourist to a little hut.
Feeping inside he will discover a bald-headed
Scotchman toasting his pet bunion at a mud
volcano and smoking his pipe serenely. Months
ago he suffered from auricular rheumatism. All
other remedies failing, he went fur the sulphurous
mud-baths with such effect that the flies now sit
on the dog-leg-fence outside and curse him with
a regretful buzz. Having such faith in the geysei,
he erected this hut close by, and found the place
mighty convenient for household requirements,
especially in case he required hot water for either
his feet or his tea. His sulphurous tranquillity
was rudely disturbed one morning to find that his
pet volcano had shifted its position had taken a
lease of his front room, and was playing up
with unabated vigour near his bunk. An ordinary
mind would have looked on this as a distinct
notice to quit. His didn’t. He saw at a glance
the saving to be effected in labour, etc., and imine-
diatelv dug a hole to allow the geyser fair play.
To see him sitting there making his porridge or
boiling a clean rag for Sunday is the least let
gruesome, especially when the gurgling, hissing
noises are blended with suffocating sulphurous
smells, reminding one forcibly of Bheol and old
Nick.
